### transformers — 🟡 MEDIUM

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE` — trust_remote_code=True Usage Without Sufficient Safeguards
  > The SKILL.md instructions mention using 'trust_remote_code=True' when loading models with custom architectures. This parameter allows arbitrary Python code from the Hugging Face Hub to execute in the user's environment. While the instructions note this should only be used 'when the model card requires custom code you have reviewed', the guidance is insufficiently strong and could lead users to enable arbitrary remote code execution without adequate review.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add a prominent security warning about the risks of trust_remote_code=True. Recommend users inspect the model repository's source code before enabling this flag, and suggest using model hashes/revisions to pin to a specific reviewed version (e.g., from_pretrained('model-id', trust_remote_code=True, revision='specific-commit-hash')).

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — HF_TOKEN Environment Variable Guidance Could Encourage Insecure Practices
  > The SKILL.md instructions include guidance on setting HF_TOKEN via 'export HF_TOKEN="..."' in the environment. While the instructions do warn against committing tokens to git or shell profiles, the inline export example could be misused. The static analyzer flagged environment variable access patterns in conjunction with network calls across multiple files, though no actual exfiltration code was found in the reviewed content.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Reinforce the recommendation to use 'hf auth login' as the primary authentication method. Consider removing the inline export example or replacing it with a reference to a secrets manager pattern only.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Missing allowed-tools Declaration
  > The skill does not specify an 'allowed-tools' field in its YAML manifest. While this field is optional per the agent skills spec, its absence means there are no declared restrictions on which agent tools (Read, Write, Bash, Python, etc.) this skill may invoke. Given the skill's scope (model loading, training, inference), explicit tool declarations would improve transparency.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add an explicit 'allowed-tools' field to the YAML frontmatter, e.g., 'allowed-tools: [Python, Bash]', to document the intended tool usage scope.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Dependency Versions in Installation Instructions
  > The installation instructions use version range specifiers (e.g., 'transformers[torch]>=5.9') rather than exact pinned versions. This could allow installation of future versions with breaking changes or, in a supply chain attack scenario, a maliciously published version that satisfies the constraint.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Consider pinning exact versions (e.g., 'transformers[torch]==5.9.x') or providing a requirements.txt/lockfile with pinned hashes for reproducible and secure installations.

### xlsx — 🟡 MEDIUM

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Installation Instructions
  > The SKILL.md instructions recommend installing packages using `uv pip install openpyxl pandas` and optionally `uv pip install python-calamine` and `uv pip install defusedxml` without version pins. Unpinned installations are vulnerable to supply chain attacks where a malicious version of a package could be published and automatically installed.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin all package versions explicitly, e.g., `uv pip install openpyxl==3.1.5 pandas==2.2.2 defusedxml==0.7.1`. The SKILL.md already mentions openpyxl 3.1.5 in the best practices section — apply this pinning to the installation commands as well.

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — Dynamic Compilation and LD_PRELOAD Injection of Native Shim
  > The `soffice.py` script dynamically compiles a C source file (`_SHIM_SOURCE`) using `gcc` and loads the resulting shared library via `LD_PRELOAD` when Unix domain sockets are unavailable. While the C source is hardcoded within the Python file and a SHA-256 hash check is performed, this pattern introduces significant risk: (1) the compiled `.so` is written to a user-writable cache directory (`~/.cache/xlsx-skill/lo-shim/`), (2) `LD_PRELOAD` injects into all subsequent LibreOffice process calls, and (3) if an attacker can replace the `.so` file or the hash file before the integrity check, arbitrary native code executes with the process's privileges. The shim intercepts `socket`, `listen`, `accept`, `close`, and `read` syscalls.
  > File: `scripts/office/soffice.py`
  > **Remediation:** Consider shipping the pre-compiled shim as part of the skill package rather than compiling at runtime. If runtime compilation is necessary, verify the integrity of the compiled output (not just the source hash), use a more isolated temp directory with stricter permissions, and document this behavior prominently in the skill manifest. Validate that `gcc` is the expected binary before invoking it.

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Environment Variable Filtering in LibreOffice Subprocess Environment
  > The `get_soffice_env()` function in `scripts/office/soffice.py` constructs a minimal environment for LibreOffice subprocesses by selectively copying specific keys from `os.environ`. While the intent is to restrict secrets, the function copies `HOME`, `USER`, `PATH`, `LANG`, `LC_ALL`, `LC_CTYPE`, `TMPDIR`, `TMP`, and `TEMP` — some of which (e.g., `HOME`, `USER`) could contain or expose user identity information. The static analyzer flagged a cross-file env var exfiltration chain involving this file. However, the environment is passed only to `soffice` (LibreOffice), a local process, and no network exfiltration endpoint is present in the reviewed code. The risk is moderate: if the LibreOffice binary or macro were compromised, these env vars would be available to it.
  > File: `scripts/office/soffice.py`
  > **Remediation:** This is largely acceptable design. However, consider documenting explicitly why each env var is needed. Remove `USER` if not strictly required by LibreOffice. Ensure no additional env vars are added to `_SOFFICE_ENV_KEYS` without review.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — Subprocess Calls with User-Controlled File Paths
  > In `scripts/recalc.py`, the `recalc()` function passes a user-supplied filename directly to a `subprocess.run()` call as a positional argument to `soffice`. While this is passed as a list (not a shell string), the absolute path is constructed from user input and passed to LibreOffice. A maliciously crafted filename (e.g., containing LibreOffice macro URI syntax) could potentially influence LibreOffice's behavior. The `abs_path` is derived from `Path(filename).absolute()` without sanitization of the filename content itself.
  > File: `scripts/recalc.py`
  > **Remediation:** Validate that the filename has an expected extension (.xlsx, .xlsm) and does not contain special characters before passing to subprocess. Consider using `Path.resolve()` and checking the resolved path is within an expected working directory.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE` — LibreOffice Macro Written and Executed from User-Writable Directory
  > The `setup_libreoffice_macro()` function writes a StarBasic macro (`Module1.xba`) to the LibreOffice user macro directory (`~/.config/libreoffice/4/user/basic/Standard/` on Linux). This macro is then invoked via `soffice --headless` with a `vnd.sun.star.script:` URI. If an attacker can write to this directory before the skill does, or modify the macro file between the existence check and execution, a malicious macro could be executed by LibreOffice with the user's privileges. The existence check (`os.path.exists` + string match) is not atomic.
  > File: `scripts/recalc.py`
  > **Remediation:** Consider verifying the full content of the macro file (e.g., hash comparison) rather than just checking for a substring. Set restrictive file permissions on the macro file after writing. Document this behavior in the skill manifest so users are aware a macro is being installed in their LibreOffice profile.

### zarr-python — 🟡 MEDIUM

- **🟡 MEDIUM** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Cloud Credential Environment Variable Access Patterns
  > The skill's instructions explicitly reference AWS and GCS credential environment variables (AWS_ACCESS_KEY_ID, AWS_SECRET_ACCESS_KEY, AWS_DEFAULT_REGION, GOOGLE_APPLICATION_CREDENTIALS) and instructs the agent to use them for cloud storage operations. While the stated purpose is legitimate cloud I/O, the static analyzer flagged BEHAVIOR_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION and BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION across 2 files, indicating that Python files in the package (not shown in the provided content) may be reading these credentials and potentially transmitting them externally. The combination of credential access patterns in instructions plus static detection of exfiltration behavior is a medium-severity concern.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Review all Python files in the package for any code that reads AWS_ACCESS_KEY_ID, AWS_SECRET_ACCESS_KEY, GOOGLE_APPLICATION_CREDENTIALS, or similar credential environment variables and makes outbound network calls to non-AWS/GCS endpoints. Ensure cloud credentials are only passed to the intended cloud provider SDKs (s3fs, gcsfs) and not to third-party or attacker-controlled endpoints.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Missing Referenced Script Files (dask.py, zarr.py, h5py.py, xarray.py)
  > The skill references several Python files (dask.py, zarr.py, h5py.py, xarray.py) that were not found in the package. While these may be legitimate missing bundled files, their absence combined with the static analyzer's detection of environment variable exfiltration and cross-file exfiltration chains across 2 files warrants attention. The static analyzer flagged BEHAVIOR_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION and BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_EXFILTRATION_CHAIN, suggesting that among the 5 Python files detected in the package inventory, some may contain credential harvesting or data exfiltration logic that was not surfaced in the provided content.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Audit all 5 Python files detected in the package inventory. Verify that none of them read environment variables (AWS_ACCESS_KEY_ID, AWS_SECRET_ACCESS_KEY, GOOGLE_APPLICATION_CREDENTIALS, etc.) and transmit them to external endpoints. The static analyzer's cross-file exfiltration chain finding is a significant red flag requiring manual review of the unreported Python files.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Third-Party Community Skill Impersonating Official Zarr Package
  > The skill explicitly states it is 'a community guide maintained by K-Dense Inc., not an official zarr-developers package' but uses the name `zarr-python` which closely mirrors the official PyPI package name. This could cause confusion and lead users or agents to trust the skill as if it were official. The skill-author metadata ('K-Dense Inc.') is an unknown third party with no established reputation in the zarr ecosystem.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Rename the skill to clearly distinguish it from the official package (e.g., `kdense-zarr-guide`). Users should verify the provenance of community skills before installation, especially those with names matching popular open-source packages.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Dependency Versions in Installation Instructions
  > The skill recommends installing zarr with a version range (`zarr>=3.2,<4`) rather than an exact pinned version. Similarly, `s3fs` and `gcsfs` are installed without any version pins. This exposes users to supply chain risks where a malicious or compromised package version could be automatically installed.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin all dependencies to exact versions (e.g., `zarr==3.2.1`, `s3fs==2024.x.x`, `gcsfs==2024.x.x`) and verify package hashes. Use a lockfile approach for reproducible installs.

### adaptyv — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION` — Skill References External URLs for Authoritative Instructions
  > The SKILL.md instructs the agent to consult external documentation sources including `https://docs.adaptyvbio.com/api-reference`, `https://docs.adaptyvbio.com/llms.txt`, and `https://foundry-api-public.adaptyvbio.com/api/v1/openapi.json`. If these external resources were compromised or modified, they could deliver malicious instructions to the agent. The `llms.txt` index in particular is designed to be consumed by LLMs and could contain prompt injection payloads if the external server were compromised.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Treat content fetched from external URLs as untrusted data. If the agent fetches and processes `llms.txt` or the OpenAPI spec at runtime, it should sanitize and validate the content before acting on it, and should not follow any instruction-like content found in those external resources.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Overly Broad Activation Triggers in Skill Description
  > The skill description contains an extensive list of activation triggers including generic code patterns (`adaptyv`, `adaptyv_sdk`, `FoundryClient`), domain references (`foundry-api-public.adaptyvbio.com`), and broad topic areas (protein binding assays, BLI/SPR assays, thermostability assays). While these are legitimate use cases, the breadth of triggers could cause the skill to activate in contexts where it is not needed, potentially interfering with other skills or workflows.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Narrow the activation triggers to the most specific and unambiguous identifiers (e.g., explicit Adaptyv brand mentions or SDK imports) rather than broad scientific domain terms that could match unrelated contexts.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — SDK Installed from Unpinned GitHub Source Without Version Pin
  > The skill instructs users to install the `adaptyv-sdk` package directly from a GitHub repository without specifying a commit hash, tag, or version pin. This means any future commit to the repository (including potentially malicious ones) would be installed by users following these instructions. The skill explicitly notes the package is in beta (0.1.0) and not yet on PyPI, increasing the risk of supply chain compromise.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin the installation to a specific commit hash or tag, e.g.: `uv pip install "git+https://github.com/adaptyvbio/adaptyv-sdk.git@v0.1.0"` or `uv pip install "git+https://github.com/adaptyvbio/adaptyv-sdk.git@<commit-sha>"`. Once the package is published to PyPI with a stable release, prefer `uv pip install adaptyv-sdk==0.1.0`.

### benchling-integration — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Environment Variable Access Combined with Network Calls
  > The skill reads environment variables (BENCHLING_TENANT_URL, BENCHLING_API_KEY, BENCHLING_CLIENT_ID, BENCHLING_CLIENT_SECRET) and makes network calls to the Benchling API. The static analyzer flagged this pattern across multiple files as potential env var exfiltration. However, in context, the skill explicitly documents scoped reads of only named keys and instructs never to dump or iterate over the full environment. The network calls target the user's own Benchling tenant URL (user-controlled). This is expected behavior for a Benchling integration skill, but the pattern warrants documentation as a low-severity informational finding since the tenant URL itself is user-supplied and could theoretically be redirected.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** The skill already includes good guidance: read only named environment variables, never iterate os.environ, and route calls exclusively to the tenant URL. Users should verify the BENCHLING_TENANT_URL is set to their own tenant before running integrations. No code changes required, but consider adding runtime validation that the tenant URL matches an expected domain pattern (e.g., *.benchling.com).

  > The skill documents installation of benchling-sdk with a pinned stable version (1.25.0), which is good practice. However, it also documents a prerelease installation command without version pinning ('uv pip install "benchling-sdk" --prerelease allow'). Unpinned prerelease installs could pull in untested or potentially compromised package versions from PyPI.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add a warning that prerelease installs should never be used in production and should specify a version pin even for preview builds (e.g., 'uv pip install "benchling-sdk==1.26.0a1" --prerelease allow'). The existing note '(alpha; not for production)' is helpful but insufficient to prevent accidental use.

  > The skill references two Python files (benchling_sdk.py and Bio.py) that were not found in the package. The Bio.py reference corresponds to the BioPython library (from Bio import SeqIO), which is an external dependency used in the bulk entity import example. The benchling_sdk.py reference appears to be an artifact. Missing files mean the skill cannot be fully audited for supply chain risks, and the BioPython dependency (biopython package) is not pinned in any manifest.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Remove the spurious benchling_sdk.py and Bio.py file references. Document the biopython dependency explicitly with a pinned version (e.g., 'uv pip install biopython==1.84') in the skill's setup instructions to prevent supply chain risks from unpinned transitive dependencies.

  > The static analyzer detected a cross-file exfiltration chain pattern spanning 8 files and env var access across 7 files. Upon review, all instances in the reference files (references/authentication.md, references/sdk_reference.md, references/eventbridge.md) are documentation examples showing legitimate Benchling API usage patterns. The Lambda handler in references/eventbridge.md reads BENCHLING_TENANT_URL and BENCHLING_API_KEY and makes API calls, which is the intended integration pattern. No actual exfiltration to third-party endpoints was found. This is flagged as low severity due to the volume of env var + network call combinations that could be misused if the reference code is copied without review.

### bids — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Overly Broad Skill Description May Cause Unintended Activation
  > The skill description enumerates a very wide range of modalities and tasks (MRI, EEG, MEG, iEEG, PET, microscopy, NIRS, motion capture, EMG, MR spectroscopy, behavioral, DICOM conversion, validation, metadata writing, derivatives). While this accurately reflects the skill's scope, the breadth may cause the skill to be activated for loosely related queries, potentially running the update_schema.py script (which makes network calls) in contexts where the user did not intend external network access.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** This is a minor concern given the skill's legitimate broad scope. Consider noting in the description that the update_schema.py script requires explicit user invocation and makes network calls, so users are aware of this behavior.

- **⚪ INFO** `LLM_CONTEXT_BUDGET_EXCEEDED` — 'references/bids_schema.json' excluded from LLM analysis (813,726 chars)
  > file size (813,726 chars) exceeds per-file limit (75,000)
  > File: `references/bids_schema.json`
  > **Remediation:** Increase llm_analysis.max_referenced_file_chars in your scan policy to include this content in LLM analysis.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION` — External Content Written to Local Reference Files Without Integrity Verification
  > The update_schema.py script downloads content from external URLs and writes it directly to local reference files (references/bids_schema.json, references/beps.yml) that are subsequently used by the skill as authoritative reference data. If the upstream sources are compromised or a malicious URL is supplied, the downloaded content could contain adversarial instructions or malformed data that influences the agent's behavior when it reads these reference files.
  > File: `scripts/update_schema.py`
  > **Remediation:** Add cryptographic hash verification of downloaded files against known-good checksums. Consider pinning to specific tagged releases rather than 'stable' or 'main' branch URLs. Treat downloaded content as untrusted and validate structure before writing.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Network Calls in Schema Update Script
  > The update_schema.py script makes outbound HTTP requests to fetch data from external URLs (bids-specification.readthedocs.io and raw.githubusercontent.com). While these are legitimate upstream BIDS sources, the script uses urllib.request.urlopen without certificate verification enforcement or integrity checking (no hash verification of downloaded content). The --schema-url argument also allows arbitrary URLs to be passed, meaning an agent could be instructed to fetch schema from an attacker-controlled URL.
  > File: `scripts/update_schema.py:30`
  > **Remediation:** Add SSL certificate verification (it is enabled by default in Python's urllib but should be explicitly confirmed). Add content integrity verification (e.g., checksum comparison against known-good values). Restrict --schema-url to an allowlist of trusted domains rather than accepting arbitrary URLs.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — Arbitrary URL Injection via --schema-url Argument
  > The update_schema.py script accepts a --schema-url command-line argument that is passed directly to urllib.request.urlopen without domain validation. If an agent or user supplies a malicious URL (e.g., a local file:// URI, an internal network address, or an attacker-controlled server), the script will fetch and write that content to references/bids_schema.json, potentially introducing malicious schema data or enabling SSRF-like behavior within the agent's environment.
  > File: `scripts/update_schema.py:57`
  > **Remediation:** Validate the --schema-url argument against an allowlist of trusted domains (e.g., bids-specification.readthedocs.io, raw.githubusercontent.com). Reject file://, ftp://, or non-HTTPS schemes. Consider removing the --schema-url argument entirely if dynamic URL override is not needed.

  > The skill instructs the agent to use curl via shell/bash tools for POST-only APIs, and to URL-encode user-supplied parameters such as SMILES strings, compound names, gene symbols, and ontology terms.   > The skill instructs the agent to read API keys from shell environment variables (e.g., $FRED_API_KEY, $NASA_API_KEY, etc.) and from a .env file in the current working directory. While this is a common and legitimate pattern, it means the agent will actively read potentially sensitive credentials from the environment and local filesystem as part of normal operation. The skill covers 17+ API keys across multiple services. If the skill is invoked in an unexpected context, it could expose these credentials.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** This is standard practice but users should be aware that the skill reads environment variables and .env files. Ensure the .env file has appropriate filesystem permissions and is not world-readable. Consider documenting which specific variables are accessed.

  > The skill instructs the agent to return 'raw JSON' responses from external databases and to 'default to showing the full raw JSON.' If any of the 78 external databases returns maliciously crafted JSON containing instruction-like text, the agent may process those instructions. This is a standard indirect prompt injection risk inherent to any skill that fetches and displays external content verbatim.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Consider adding a note to sanitize or clearly delimit external API responses before presenting them to the user. Instruct the agent to treat all API response content as data, not instructions, and to not follow any directives found within API responses.

The phrase 'any public database API query' is an extremely broad activation trigger that could cause the skill to be invoked for nearly any information lookup task, potentially displacing more appropriate tools or skills.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Narrow the activation description to specific database categories rather than using catch-all phrases like 'any public database API query'. List specific use cases to avoid over-broad activation.

  > The skill instructs the agent to read molecular data files from user-provided URLs (e.g., https://example.com/data.csv, s3://bucket/compounds.sdf). If an attacker controls the remote file content, they could embed malicious instructions within CSV/SDF metadata fields, molecule names, or comments that the agent might process as instructions. The skill does not instruct the agent to treat content from remote files as untrusted data only.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add explicit guidance that content read from external URLs must be treated as untrusted data and should not be interpreted as instructions. Instruct the agent to sanitize or ignore non-chemical metadata fields from externally sourced files before processing.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Multiple Non-Existent Referenced Files Inflate Apparent Capability
  > The skill references numerous files that do not exist in the package (scipy.py, sklearn.py, datamol.py, rdkit.py, templates/*, assets/*, =[O:2]). While most appear to be documentation artifacts or parser artifacts (=[O:2] is a SMARTS fragment misidentified as a file reference), the presence of many missing referenced files could mislead the agent about available resources and cause it to attempt to load non-existent local files. The file =[O:2] is particularly anomalous and suggests a parser/extraction artifact.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Remove references to non-existent files from the skill package or add the actual files. Clarify in the instructions that scipy, sklearn, rdkit, and datamol are PyPI packages (not local files), which the skill already partially does but should make more explicit to avoid agent confusion.

### deepchem — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Missing Referenced Script Files May Indicate Incomplete Package
  > Several files referenced in the SKILL.md instructions are not present in the skill package: templates/workflows.md, templates/api_reference.md, assets/api_reference.md, sklearn.py, deepchem.py, assets/workflows.md. While the core reference files (references/api_reference.md and references/workflows.md) are present, the missing files could indicate an incomplete package or that some referenced functionality is unavailable. This is a low-severity informational finding as it does not represent a direct security threat but could cause unexpected behavior.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Ensure all referenced files are included in the skill package or remove references to non-existent files from the instructions.

  > The SKILL.md manifest does not specify 'allowed-tools' or 'compatibility' fields. ### esm — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Overly Broad Skill Description May Trigger Unintended Activation
  > The skill description is very broad: 'Comprehensive toolkit for EvolutionaryScale protein language models including ESM3 (generative multimodal design across sequence, structure, and function) and ESM C (efficient embeddings). Use for protein sequence/structure/function tasks, inverse folding, embeddings, variant design, and ESMFold2 structure prediction via Biohub.' This enumerates many trigger keywords (sequence, structure, function, inverse folding, embeddings, variant design, ESMFold2) which could cause the skill to be activated for a wide range of protein-related queries, including those better handled by other tools. This is a minor concern given the legitimate scientific scope.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Consider scoping the description more precisely to the specific tasks this skill handles, reducing the breadth of activation triggers. This is a minor issue for a legitimate scientific toolkit.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned GitHub Dependency Install in Reference Documentation
  > The biohub-platform.md reference file includes an installation command that installs a package directly from a GitHub repository with only a short commit hash (c94ed8d) as a pin. While a commit hash is better than a branch name, the referenced GitHub organization 'Biohub' is not the primary EvolutionaryScale organization, and the repository provenance is less established. If the GitHub repository were compromised or the commit hash were to resolve differently in edge cases, malicious code could be installed.
  > File: `references/biohub-platform.md`
  > **Remediation:** Prefer the PyPI-pinned version (uv pip install "esm==3.2.3") whenever possible. If the GitHub install is necessary for ESMFold2 features, document the expected SHA256 hash of the installed package and instruct users to verify it. Clearly note that this installs from a third-party GitHub organization, not the official PyPI release.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — API Key Accessed via Environment Variable for External API Calls
  > The skill reads ESM_API_KEY from environment variables and uses it to authenticate against external cloud APIs (Forge at forge.evolutionaryscale.ai and Biohub at biohub.ai). While this is a legitimate and documented pattern for API authentication, the static analyzer flagged a cross-file env var exfiltration chain. In context, the usage appears intentional and documented — the skill explicitly instructs users to set ESM_API_KEY and never hardcode tokens. The risk is low but worth noting: if the skill were compromised or if a malicious reference file were loaded, the API key could be exfiltrated to an attacker-controlled endpoint instead of the legitimate API.
  > File: `references/forge-api.md`
  > **Remediation:** The current pattern is acceptable. Ensure the API endpoint URLs (forge.evolutionaryscale.ai, biohub.ai) are validated and not configurable via user input. Consider adding explicit documentation that users should verify the endpoint URLs have not been tampered with before running the skill.

### exploratory-data-analysis — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Missing allowed-tools Declaration
  > The skill does not declare an allowed-tools field in the YAML manifest. The skill executes Python scripts that read files, access the filesystem, and potentially make library calls. Without an explicit allowed-tools declaration, there is no manifest-level constraint on what tools the agent can use when executing this skill.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add an explicit allowed-tools field to the YAML manifest listing only the tools required: e.g., allowed-tools: [Python, Read, Glob]. This provides a clear security boundary.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Over-Broad Capability Claims in Skill Description
  > The skill description claims support for '200+ file formats' across six major scientific domains. While the reference files do cover many formats, the breadth of the claim ('This skill should be used when analyzing any scientific data file') could lead to over-activation of the skill for files it cannot actually handle, potentially causing unexpected behavior or errors. The description uses activation-maximizing language ('should be used when analyzing any scientific data file') that could cause the agent to invoke this skill unnecessarily broadly.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Narrow the description to accurately reflect actual capabilities. Avoid absolute language like 'any scientific data file'. Specify limitations and unsupported formats clearly.

### generate-image — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — API Key Read from Environment/.env File and Transmitted to External API
  > The script reads the OPENROUTER_API_KEY from .env files (traversing up the directory tree) or environment variables and transmits it as a Bearer token in HTTP requests to https://openrouter.ai. While this is the intended and documented behavior for authenticating with the OpenRouter API, the .env traversal walks all parent directories which could inadvertently pick up API keys from unrelated projects. The static analyzer flagged cross-file env var exfiltration chains, likely due to the combination of env var reading and network calls.
  > File: `scripts/generate_image.py`
  > **Remediation:** Limit .env file search to the current directory and at most one parent directory rather than traversing all the way to the filesystem root. This prevents accidentally picking up API keys from unrelated projects higher in the directory tree.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Input Image File Read and Transmitted to External API
  > When an input image is provided via --input, the script reads the file from disk, base64-encodes it, and sends it to the OpenRouter API. There is no validation of the file path to prevent path traversal (e.g., --input ../../../../etc/passwd or sensitive files). A user or automated workflow could inadvertently pass sensitive files as input images. The content would be sent to the external OpenRouter API.
  > File: `scripts/generate_image.py`
  > **Remediation:** Validate that the input image path resolves to an expected directory (e.g., within the current working directory). Check that the file extension matches an expected image type before reading. Reject paths containing '..' or absolute paths outside the working directory.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned 'requests' Dependency
  > The script imports the 'requests' library without any version pinning or requirements file specifying an exact version. If a requirements.txt or similar dependency manifest is used, an unpinned 'requests' package could be substituted with a malicious version via supply chain compromise or dependency confusion attacks.
  > File: `scripts/generate_image.py:108`
  > **Remediation:** Include a requirements.txt with a pinned version (e.g., requests==2.32.3) and verify package integrity using hashes (pip install --require-hashes).

### geopandas — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Missing allowed-tools Declaration
  > The SKILL.md manifest does not declare an allowed-tools field. While this field is optional per the agent skills specification, its absence means there are no declared restrictions on which agent tools this skill may use. Given that the skill's instructions include patterns for file I/O, network access (PostGIS, remote URLs, cloud storage), and code execution, declaring allowed-tools would improve transparency and reduce the risk of unintended capability use.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add an explicit allowed-tools declaration to the YAML frontmatter listing the tools actually needed (e.g., Python, Bash for executing geospatial code). This improves auditability and limits unintended tool use.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Hardcoded Database Credentials in Documentation Examples
  > The data-io.md reference file contains example code showing database connection strings with placeholder credentials (user:password@host:port/database). While these are documentation examples and not real credentials, agents following these instructions might be prompted to fill in real credentials, and the pattern normalizes embedding credentials directly in connection strings rather than using environment variables or secrets managers.
  > File: `references/data-io.md`
  > **Remediation:** Update documentation examples to demonstrate credential best practices, such as using environment variables: create_engine(f'postgresql://{os.environ["DB_USER"]}:{os.environ["DB_PASSWORD"]}@{host}/{db}') or using a secrets manager.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION` — Skill Instructs Reading Data from External URLs Without Validation
  > The data-io.md reference file documents reading spatial data directly from URLs (HTTP/HTTPS, S3, Azure Blob Storage) without any guidance on validating the source or content. An agent following these instructions could be directed by a user to load data from a malicious URL containing crafted geospatial data or embedded instructions. The risk is moderate in that malformed or malicious geospatial files could exploit parsing vulnerabilities in underlying libraries (GDAL/Fiona/Pyogrio).
  > File: `references/data-io.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add guidance to validate URLs against an allowlist before reading, warn users about risks of loading data from untrusted external sources, and recommend scanning or sandboxing external geospatial files before processing.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — Use of eval/exec in Python Code Blocks
  > Static analysis flagged two instances of eval/exec usage in Python code blocks within the markdown documentation files. Upon review, these appear to be within legitimate GeoPandas/Shapely usage examples (e.g., affine_transform calls and similar operations) rather than direct injection vectors. However, if an agent were to execute these code blocks verbatim with user-supplied input substituted into the parameters, it could lead to code injection. The risk is low given the educational/reference context, but worth noting.
  > File: `references/geometric-operations.md`
  > **Remediation:** Ensure that any user-supplied values passed to geometric transformation functions are validated and sanitized before use. Avoid directly substituting untrusted user input into affine_transform or similar function calls without type checking and bounds validation.

### get-available-resources — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — Static Analyzer Flagged eval/exec Usage in Markdown Code Blocks
  > The pre-scan static analyzer flagged two instances of Python eval/exec usage in markdown code blocks within SKILL.md. Review of the instruction body shows these are example code snippets for user guidance (e.g., reading JSON, using joblib/torch), not executable code run by the skill itself. No actual eval/exec calls were found in the detect_resources.py script. This is a low-severity informational finding based on the static analyzer alert.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Review the markdown code blocks to confirm no eval/exec patterns are present in executable context. If the static analyzer is triggering on benign patterns (e.g., string literals containing 'exec'), this can be documented as a false positive.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Missing allowed-tools Manifest Declaration
  > The SKILL.md YAML frontmatter does not declare an `allowed-tools` field. The skill executes Python scripts and runs subprocess commands (nvidia-smi, rocm-smi, sysctl, system_profiler), writes files to disk, and reads system information. Declaring allowed tools improves transparency and enables enforcement of capability boundaries.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add `allowed-tools: [Python, Bash]` to the YAML frontmatter to explicitly declare the tools this skill requires, improving transparency and enabling tool restriction enforcement.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — Subprocess Calls to External System Utilities
  > The script invokes external system utilities (nvidia-smi, rocm-smi, sysctl, system_profiler) via subprocess.run(). While these are standard system tools and the calls use fixed argument lists (no user-controlled input), the use of subprocess with external binaries carries inherent risk if the PATH is manipulated or if a malicious binary shadows these tool names. All calls use timeout and capture_output, which is good practice.
  > File: `scripts/detect_resources.py:90`
  > **Remediation:** Use absolute paths for system utilities where possible (e.g., /usr/bin/nvidia-smi) or validate the resolved path before execution. This reduces risk from PATH manipulation attacks.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — System Information Disclosure via JSON Output File
  > The skill collects detailed system information including CPU architecture, processor model, memory details, disk usage, GPU information (including driver versions and compute capabilities), and OS version, then writes this to a `.claude_resources.json` file in the current working directory. While this is the stated purpose of the skill, the aggregated hardware fingerprint could be sensitive in certain environments. The file persists on disk and could be read by other processes or inadvertently committed to version control.
  > File: `scripts/detect_resources.py:180`
  > **Remediation:** Consider adding a note in the skill documentation recommending users add `.claude_resources.json` to `.gitignore` to prevent accidental exposure of system fingerprint data. Optionally, support an in-memory-only mode that does not persist the file.

  > The SKILL.md references a file 'gget.py' in its instructions, but this file was not found in the skill package.   > The installation instructions use 'uv pip install --upgrade gget' without pinning to a specific version.   > The skill manifest does not declare an 'allowed-tools' field. The scripts use file I/O, network calls (via gget API calls to external databases), and subprocess-level operations. While omission of allowed-tools is informational per spec, the absence means no tool restrictions are enforced for an agent executing these scripts.
  > File: `SKILL.md`

### gtars — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Installation Without Version Constraints
  > The skill instructs installation of 'gtars' via 'uv pip install gtars' and 'cargo install gtars-cli' without specifying pinned versions. This means the agent could install any version of the package, including a potentially compromised future release or a typosquatted package. No version pinning, hash verification, or integrity checks are specified.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin package versions explicitly (e.g., 'uv pip install gtars==0.1.x') and verify package integrity via checksums or lockfiles. For Cargo, specify exact versions in Cargo.toml.

  > The skill manifest does not specify a license or compatibility field. The 'skill-author' is listed as 'K-Dense Inc.' but no version provenance or trust chain is established. Missing metadata reduces auditability and makes it harder to assess supply chain trustworthiness.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add explicit license (e.g., 'license: MIT'), compatibility, and version fields to the YAML manifest to improve auditability and trust assessment.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE` — Unbounded Parallel Processing and Memory Options Without Limits
  > The Python API reference exposes options for parallel processing and memory usage without enforcing upper bounds. Instructions like 'gtars.set_option("parallel.threads", 4)' and 'gtars --memory-limit 4G' could be set to arbitrarily high values by a user or automated workflow, potentially exhausting system resources. The streaming and memory-mapped file support for 'large files' also lacks explicit safeguards.
  > File: `references/python-api.md`
  > **Remediation:** Document recommended safe limits for thread counts and memory usage. Consider adding validation in workflows to cap resource consumption to reasonable defaults appropriate for the execution environment.

### hypogenic — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — Lambda-Based Dynamic Code Execution via extract_label Parameter
  > The skill's Python API accepts a user-supplied lambda or function as the 'extract_label' parameter passed directly to BaseTask. While this is a legitimate API design pattern, it means arbitrary Python code can be passed as a callable and executed during inference. If the skill is used in an automated pipeline where the config or extract_label function originates from untrusted sources, this could be exploited for code injection. The static analyzer also flagged a Python code block using eval/exec patterns.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Document clearly that the extract_label function should only accept trusted, user-authored code. If the skill is used in automated pipelines, validate that the callable source is trusted before execution. Avoid accepting extract_label definitions from external or user-provided configuration files.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Installation via uv pip install
  > The skill instructs users to install the 'hypogenic' package without pinning a specific version (e.g., 'uv pip install hypogenic'). This means any future malicious or compromised version published to PyPI could be installed automatically, creating a supply chain risk. Additionally, the skill clones external GitHub repositories (ChicagoHAI/HypoGeniC-datasets, ChicagoHAI/Hypothesis-agent-datasets) without specifying commit hashes or tags, meaning repository content could change unexpectedly.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin the package to a specific version (e.g., 'uv pip install hypogenic==1.0.0'). For git clones, use a specific commit hash or tag (e.g., 'git clone ... && git checkout <commit-hash>') to ensure reproducible and auditable installations.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Missing Referenced Script Files
  > The skill references several files in its instructions that do not exist in the package: examples.py, templates/config_template.yaml, assets/config_template.yaml, and hypogenic.py. While references/config_template.yaml was found, the missing files could indicate an incomplete package or that users will be directed to fetch these from external sources, creating a potential supply chain gap.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Ensure all referenced files are included in the skill package. If files are intentionally omitted (e.g., generated at runtime), document this clearly. Avoid referencing files that users might attempt to source from untrusted external locations.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — API Key Stored in Environment Variable Referenced in Config Template
  > The configuration template references an environment variable 'OPENAI_API_KEY' for API authentication. While using environment variables is better than hardcoding secrets, the config template also shows the variable name explicitly, and the skill instructs users to configure API keys this way. If config files are committed to version control or shared, the environment variable name and usage pattern is exposed. More importantly, the skill makes outbound API calls to OpenAI/Anthropic endpoints with user data, which constitutes data leaving the local environment.
  > File: `references/config_template.yaml:14`
  > **Remediation:** Ensure users are aware that dataset content (potentially sensitive research data) is transmitted to external LLM APIs (OpenAI, Anthropic). Document data privacy implications clearly. Advise against committing config files containing API key environment variable names to public repositories.

  > The setup-deployment reference file includes explicit instructions for handling AWS and GCP credentials, including setting environment variables with access keys and secret keys. If a user asks the agent to help debug credential issues, the agent may be guided to read and display these sensitive values.

  > Multiple reference files contain database connection string patterns with plaintext passwords embedded directly in the URI format (e.g., postgresql://user:password@hostname:5432/dbname). The skill instructs the agent to help users configure these connection strings, which could lead the agent to handle or display plaintext database credentials during setup assistance.

  > **Remediation:** Add explicit warnings in the skill instructions that actual passwords should never be placed in connection strings shown to the agent. Recommend using environment variables or secrets managers for database credentials, and instruct the agent to redact any passwords it encounters in connection strings.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Installation Instructions
  > The setup and integration reference files recommend installing packages using pip without version pinning (e.g., 'pip install lamindb', 'pip install lamindb[gcp,zarr,fcs]'). Unpinned installations are vulnerable to supply chain attacks where a malicious package version could be installed. The skill may instruct the agent to run these unpinned install commands on behalf of users.
  > File: `references/setup-deployment.md`
  > **Remediation:** Update installation instructions to recommend pinned versions (e.g., 'pip install lamindb==0.x.y'). Encourage use of lock files (requirements.txt with pinned versions or pyproject.toml with exact versions) and recommend verifying package integrity via checksums.

  > Several files referenced in SKILL.md instructions are not present in the skill package: liteparse.py, assets/choosing_a_parser.md, assets/cli_reference.md, assets/api_reference.md, assets/output_formats.md, assets/ocr_and_formats.md, templates/api_reference.md, templates/ocr_and_formats.md, templates/output_formats.md, templates/cli_reference.md, templates/choosing_a_parser.md.   > The skill description explicitly instructs the agent to use liteparse 'even when the user does not name liteparse', which is a capability inflation / keyword baiting pattern designed to increase unwanted or unsolicited activation of this skill. This manipulates the agent's skill-selection logic to prefer this skill over alternatives without explicit user intent.
  > File: `SKILL.md`

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Static Analyzer False-Positive: No Actual Exfiltration Chain Found
  > The pre-scan static analyzer flagged BEHAVIOR_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION, BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_EXFILTRATION_CHAIN, and BEHAVIOR_CROSSFILE_ENV_VAR_EXFILTRATION. Manual review of all available code (scripts/batch_parse_dir.py, references/api_reference.md, references/ocr_and_formats.md, references/cli_reference.md, references/output_formats.md, references/choosing_a_parser.md) finds no actual network calls, no environment variable harvesting for exfiltration, and no credential access. The only environment variable mentioned is TESSDATA_PREFIX, which is a standard Tesseract configuration variable used locally. The OCR server URL (ocr_server_url) is an optional user-configured local endpoint (e.g., http://localhost:8080/ocr), not a hardcoded external exfiltration target. No cross-file exfiltration chain is present in the reviewed code. This finding is informational to document the static analyzer flags were investigated and cleared.
  > File: `scripts/batch_parse_dir.py`
  > **Remediation:** No remediation required. Static analyzer flags appear to be false positives based on pattern matching. Continue monitoring if additional script files (liteparse.py, assets/* files) become available for review, as those referenced files were not found in the package.

While Bash is declared, the skill does not declare Python as an allowed tool despite the generate_market_visuals.py script using sys.executable and subprocess.run to invoke Python scripts. This could cause the skill to be activated in contexts where users are simply asking about McKinsey/BCG/Gartner reports, even when a simpler analysis would suffice. - **🔵 LOW** `LLM_RESOURCE_ABUSE` — Unbounded Visual Generation Loop - Potential Resource Exhaustion
  > The skill instructs the agent to generate 5-6 core visuals at the start of every report, plus up to 27 additional extended visuals when using --all flag. The generate_market_visuals.py script iterates over all visuals with a 120-second timeout per image. For the full set (27+ visuals), this could consume significant compute resources and time. The instructions also say 'no token constraints' and 'write fully, don't abbreviate' for 50+ page reports, which combined with batch image generation could cause resource exhaustion.
  > File: `scripts/generate_market_visuals.py:155`
  > **Remediation:** Add a maximum visual count guard and warn users before generating all 27+ visuals. Consider requiring explicit user confirmation before batch generation.

Pickle deserialization of untrusted data is a well-known arbitrary code execution vector. While this is presented as documentation rather than executable skill logic, if an agent follows these instructions on user-provided pickle files, it could lead to arbitrary code execution on the user's machine.

The code block shows: `eval('samtools --version | sed "1!d; s/samtools //"')` as a Nextflow process output directive. This is a Nextflow language construct, not arbitrary Python code execution. The risk is low but worth noting as a pattern that could be misused if a user were instructed to use eval() with untrusted input in a Nextflow script context.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Hardcoded Credentials in Example Code Blocks
  > Multiple reference files contain hardcoded credential examples (USERNAME = 'user', PASSWORD = 'pass') in complete example code blocks. While these are clearly placeholder/example values intended for documentation purposes, they establish a pattern that users might replicate with real credentials in scripts. The connection.md file explicitly notes in best practices to use environment variables, but the complete examples still show hardcoded strings.

This reinforces secure credential handling throughout all examples.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — Use of eval/exec in Python Code Blocks
  > The static analyzer flagged a potential eval/exec usage in the Python code blocks within the referenced markdown files. Reviewing the content, the code blocks in references/rois.md contain `int.from_bytes(...)` and struct operations, and references/advanced.md contains `int(s, 2)` which converts a binary string to integer. The `int(s, 2)` call in the mask creation helper function (`create_mask_bytes`) in references/rois.md processes a dynamically constructed binary string `s` built from mask byte values. While this is not a direct `eval()` or `exec()` call, the pattern of constructing a string from data and then interpreting it (`int(s, 2)`) could be flagged by static analyzers. In context, this is used for bit-packing mask data and does not represent a direct code injection risk since the input is derived from numpy array bytes, not user-supplied strings.
  > File: `references/rois.md`
  > **Remediation:** Replace the string-based bit manipulation with direct bitwise operations using numpy or struct, which avoids any string-to-integer interpretation pattern. For example, use numpy's packbits function: `np.packbits(mask_array.astype(np.uint8))` which is cleaner and avoids the flagged pattern entirely.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE` — Missing allowed-tools Declaration
  > The skill does not declare an allowed-tools field in the YAML manifest. While this field is optional per the agent skills spec, its absence means there are no declared restrictions on which agent tools this skill can use. The skill executes Python code and reads files, so declaring allowed tools would improve transparency.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add an explicit allowed-tools declaration to the YAML frontmatter, e.g., 'allowed-tools: [Python, Read]', to document the intended tool usage scope.

### optimize-for-gpu — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Missing License and Compatibility Metadata
  > The skill manifest does not specify a license or compatibility field. While this is a LOW severity informational finding, the absence of provenance metadata (license) makes it harder to assess the trustworthiness and intended scope of the skill package.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add a license field (e.g., 'license: MIT') and a compatibility field to the YAML frontmatter to improve transparency and provenance.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION` — Instruction to Read and Execute Content from Reference Files Before Writing Code
  > The SKILL.md instructions direct the agent to 'Read the specific reference before writing code' from a large set of internal reference files (references/cupy.md, references/numba.md, etc.). While these are internal skill package files (not external URLs), many of the referenced files do not exist in the package (e.g., templates/cuxfilter.md, assets/kvikio.md, scipy.py, cucim.py, etc.). If any of these missing files were to be created by a malicious actor or substituted, the agent would read and follow their instructions. The pattern of reading files and then acting on their content creates a transitive trust surface.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Audit and remove references to non-existent files. Ensure all referenced files are present in the skill package. Consider validating file contents before acting on them, especially for files in 'templates/' and 'assets/' directories that are currently missing.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Over-Broad Activation Triggers in Skill Description
  > The skill description is extremely broad, claiming to activate not only when users explicitly request GPU acceleration but also 'when you see CPU-bound Python code (loops, large arrays, ML pipelines, graph analytics, image processing) that would benefit from GPU acceleration, even if not explicitly requested.' This proactive, unsolicited activation pattern means the skill may inject itself into conversations where the user has not asked for GPU optimization, potentially overriding user intent or displacing other skills.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Restrict activation triggers to explicit user requests for GPU/CUDA acceleration. Remove the 'even if not explicitly requested' clause to prevent unsolicited skill activation.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Versions in Installation Instructions
  > All installation instructions use unpinned package names (e.g., 'uv add cupy-cuda12x', 'uv add warp-lang', 'uv add --extra-index-url=https://pypi.nvidia.com cudf-cu12'). Without version pins, the skill could inadvertently install a compromised or breaking version of a dependency if a package is updated or if a typosquatting/supply-chain attack targets these packages on PyPI or the NVIDIA index.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin package versions in installation instructions (e.g., 'uv add cupy-cuda12x==13.x.x'). At minimum, document recommended version ranges. Also note that the NVIDIA extra index URL (https://pypi.nvidia.com) is a non-default index which should be verified as trustworthy by the user.

### paper-lookup — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — API Keys Loaded from Environment and .env Files
  > The skill instructs the agent to load API keys from environment variables (NCBI_API_KEY, CORE_API_KEY, S2_API_KEY, OPENALEX_API_KEY) and fall back to a .env file in the current working directory. While this is a common and generally acceptable pattern, it means the agent will automatically read potentially sensitive credentials from the environment and local files without explicit user confirmation for each key access. The skill also instructs the agent to 'tell the user which key is missing' which could inadvertently reveal which credentials are present or absent.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Document clearly that credential access occurs. Consider prompting the user before accessing .env files. Avoid disclosing which specific keys are present or absent in user-facing messages.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION` — Indirect Prompt Injection Risk via External API Responses
  > The skill instructs the agent to return 'raw JSON' responses from external academic databases directly to the user and to the conversation context. Academic paper titles, abstracts, and metadata are user-generated content from external sources that could contain embedded prompt injection payloads. By instructing the agent to present raw API responses without sanitization, malicious content in paper titles or abstracts could attempt to manipulate the agent's behavior. The instruction 'default to showing the full raw JSON' increases this risk.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Consider parsing and presenting structured fields rather than raw JSON blobs. Apply output sanitization or at minimum warn users that raw API content is being displayed. Avoid instructing the agent to blindly trust and relay external content.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Over-Broad Capability Claims and Keyword Baiting in Description
  > The skill description contains an extensive list of trigger keywords and use cases ('Triggers on mentions of any supported database or requests like "find papers on X" or "look up this DOI"'). This is a form of keyword baiting designed to maximize activation frequency. While the skill's actual functionality appears to match the described capabilities, the explicit enumeration of trigger phrases in the description is a discovery/activation abuse pattern that could cause the skill to be invoked more broadly than necessary.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Remove explicit trigger phrase enumeration from the description. Describe capabilities factually without listing activation keywords designed to maximize invocation frequency.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_UNAUTHORIZED_TOOL_USE` — Missing Reference Files May Cause Undefined Behavior
  > The skill references numerous files in templates/ and assets/ subdirectories (e.g., templates/crossref.md, assets/pubmed.md, templates/pmc.md, etc.) that are not found in the package. The instructions explicitly state 'Read the relevant reference file before making any API call.' When these files are missing, the agent may proceed without proper guidance, potentially making malformed API calls, or may be susceptible to confusion about which endpoints and parameters to use. This is a package integrity issue.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Ensure all referenced files are included in the skill package. Audit the file manifest and add missing reference and template files, or remove references to non-existent files from the instructions.

### pathway-enrichment — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Network Access to External APIs Without User Confirmation
  > The skill makes outbound network calls to Enrichr (maayanlab.cloud), g:Profiler (biit.cs.ut.ee), and MSigDB (gsea-msigdb.org) as part of its core workflow. While this is expected and documented behavior for an enrichment analysis skill, users should be aware that gene lists (which may contain proprietary or sensitive research data) are transmitted to third-party servers. The skill does not warn users about this data transmission.

Recommend the offline gp.enrich() path with local GMT files for sensitive/proprietary gene lists. This is already partially addressed by the offline option mentioned in the docs, but should be more prominently surfaced.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — Static Analyzer False Positives: eval/exec Flags in Documentation Code Blocks
  > The static pre-scan flagged multiple MDBLOCK_PYTHON_EVAL_EXEC findings across the markdown reference files. Upon manual review, none of the Python code blocks in SKILL.md, references/gseapy.md, references/interpretation.md, or references/databases-and-gene-sets.md contain actual eval() or exec() calls. The flags appear to be false positives triggered by code patterns in documentation examples (e.g., pandas operations, gseapy API calls). No genuine command injection risk exists in the current code.
  > File: `references/databases-and-gene-sets.md`
  > **Remediation:** No action required for the false positives. As a best practice, if future script versions accept user-supplied gene-set names or file paths, validate and sanitize those inputs before passing them to subprocess or shell calls.

### pptx — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Missing License and Provenance Metadata
  > The skill declares 'Proprietary' license referencing a LICENSE.txt file, but no author, version beyond '1.0', or contact information is provided. The compatibility field is not specified. This reduces auditability and makes it harder to verify the skill's provenance and trustworthiness.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add author, contact, and compatibility metadata to the YAML frontmatter. Ensure LICENSE.txt is bundled with the skill package.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Over-Broad Skill Activation Description
  > The skill description is intentionally crafted to trigger on an extremely wide range of user inputs. It explicitly instructs the agent to activate whenever the user mentions 'deck,' 'slides,' 'presentation,' or references any .pptx filename, regardless of context. The phrase 'regardless of what they plan to do with the content afterward' and 'If a .pptx file needs to be opened, created, or touched, use this skill' are over-broad activation triggers that could cause the skill to activate in unintended contexts.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Narrow the activation criteria to specific, well-defined tasks rather than any mention of common words like 'slides' or 'deck'. Avoid 'regardless of context' language in skill descriptions.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Dependency Versions in Installation Instructions
  > The SKILL.md dependencies section installs packages without pinned versions (e.g., 'pip install markitdown[pptx]', 'pip install Pillow', 'npm install -g pptxgenjs'). Unpinned dependencies are vulnerable to supply chain attacks where a malicious version could be published and automatically installed.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin all dependencies to specific versions (e.g., 'pip install markitdown[pptx]==0.x.x', 'pip install Pillow==10.x.x', 'npm install -g pptxgenjs@3.x.x'). Consider using a lockfile (requirements.txt with hashes, package-lock.json) for reproducible installs.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_PROMPT_INJECTION` — User-Provided PPTX Files Treated as Trusted Input for XML Parsing
  > The skill unpacks user-provided PPTX files (which are ZIP archives containing XML) and parses their XML content directly. While defusedxml is used for XML parsing (which mitigates XXE attacks), the workflow involves reading and processing arbitrary XML from user-supplied files. Maliciously crafted PPTX files could contain unexpected XML structures that interact with the validation and editing logic in unintended ways. The skill does not explicitly validate or sanitize the source PPTX before processing.
  > File: `scripts/office/unpack.py`
  > **Remediation:** Add path traversal protection when extracting ZIP archives (check that extracted paths don't escape the output directory). Consider validating the PPTX structure before processing. The use of defusedxml is good practice and should be maintained throughout.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_COMMAND_INJECTION` — ZIP Path Traversal Risk in PPTX Extraction
  > The unpack.py script uses zipfile.ZipFile.extractall() without checking for path traversal in ZIP entry names. A maliciously crafted PPTX file could contain ZIP entries with paths like '../../etc/passwd' or absolute paths, potentially writing files outside the intended output directory. This is a known vulnerability class (Zip Slip).
  > File: `scripts/office/unpack.py:47`
  > **Remediation:** Before extracting, validate all ZIP entry names to ensure they don't contain path traversal sequences. Use a safe extraction pattern:

for member in zf.namelist():
    member_path = Path(output_path) / member
    if not str(member_path.resolve()).startswith(str(output_path.resolve())):
        raise ValueError(f'Path traversal detected: {member}')
    zf.extract(member, output_path)

  > The skill does not declare an 'allowed-tools' field in its YAML manifest. The skill executes Python scripts, writes files to disk, and uses pickle serialization, so documenting tool usage would improve transparency.

  > File: `SKILL.md`

Pickle deserialization of untrusted files can lead to arbitrary code execution. While the primary use case appears to be saving/loading the skill's own outputs, the workflow guide instructs users to load arbitrary pickle files, which is a security risk if those files come from untrusted sources.
  > File: `scripts/run_deseq2_analysis.py:175`
  > **Remediation:** Add a warning in the documentation that pickle files should only be loaded from trusted sources. Consider using safer serialization formats (e.g., HDF5/AnnData's native .h5ad format) for data exchange. If pickle must be used, validate file provenance before loading.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Incomplete PHI Anonymization - UIDs Not Anonymized by Default
  > The anonymize_dicom.py script leaves DICOM UIDs (StudyInstanceUID, SeriesInstanceUID, SOPInstanceUID) intact by default. These UIDs can be used to re-identify patients by cross-referencing with PACS systems or other DICOM databases. The code to anonymize UIDs is present but commented out, with a note about 'maintaining referential integrity'. This creates a risk that users may believe files are fully anonymized when they are not.
  > File: `scripts/anonymize_dicom.py`
  > **Remediation:** Enable UID anonymization by default or add a clear warning that UIDs are preserved and can be used for re-identification. Add a --preserve-uids flag for cases where referential integrity is needed, making UID anonymization the default behavior. Document this limitation prominently.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — PHI Exposure Risk in Metadata Extraction Script
  > The extract_metadata.py script extracts and displays all DICOM metadata including Protected Health Information (PHI) such as PatientName, PatientID, PatientBirthDate, PatientSex, PatientAge, PatientWeight, and other sensitive fields. The script can write this PHI to output files without any warning or sanitization. While this is the stated purpose of the tool, there is no warning to users about PHI handling obligations (HIPAA, GDPR, etc.) or guidance on secure handling of the output.
  > File: `scripts/extract_metadata.py`
  > **Remediation:** Add prominent warnings in the script output and documentation about PHI handling obligations. Consider adding a --redact-phi flag that masks sensitive fields by default, requiring explicit opt-in to display raw PHI.

### pylabrobot — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Missing allowed-tools Declaration
  > The skill does not declare an 'allowed-tools' field in its YAML manifest. While this is optional per the spec, the skill executes Python code and makes network connections (to robot hardware via USB/HTTP), so declaring allowed tools would improve transparency and security posture.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add 'allowed-tools: [Python, Bash]' or appropriate tool restrictions to the YAML frontmatter to explicitly declare what tools this skill uses.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Multiple Referenced Files Not Found
  > The skill references numerous files that do not exist in the package (templates/visualization.md, assets/resources.md, templates/resources.md, templates/material-handling.md, pylabrobot.py, assets/liquid-handling.md, templates/hardware-backends.md, templates/analytical-equipment.md, assets/visualization.md, assets/material-handling.md, templates/liquid-handling.md, assets/analytical-equipment.md, assets/hardware-backends.md). While not directly a security threat, missing files could cause the agent to seek external sources to fill gaps, potentially leading to indirect prompt injection from untrusted external content.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Ensure all referenced files are included in the skill package, or remove references to non-existent files to prevent the agent from seeking external substitutes.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Installation Recommendation
  > The skill's Quick Start section recommends installing PyLabRobot via 'uv pip install pylabrobot' without a version pin. This could expose users to supply chain risks if the package is compromised or a breaking/malicious version is published.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Recommend pinning to a specific version: 'uv pip install pylabrobot==<version>' and document the tested/verified version in the skill manifest.

The skill executes Python code, writes files, and performs MCMC sampling, which could be resource-intensive.

### pyopenms — 🔵 LOW

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_DATA_EXFILTRATION` — Missing allowed-tools Declaration
  > The skill does not declare an allowed-tools field in the YAML manifest. While this is optional per the spec, the skill instructs installation of the pyopenms package via 'uv pip install pyopenms' and executes Python code. Without an explicit allowed-tools declaration, there is no manifest-level constraint on what tools the agent may use. This is informational only.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Add an explicit allowed-tools declaration to the YAML manifest, e.g., allowed-tools: [Python, Bash], to document the intended tool scope.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SKILL_DISCOVERY_ABUSE` — Over-broad Capability Description with Competitor Redirection
  > The skill description includes a reference to a competing skill ('For simple spectral comparison and metabolite ID use matchms'), which is an unusual pattern in a skill manifest. While this could be legitimate guidance, it may also be an attempt to manipulate skill discovery and routing behavior by embedding competitor-steering language in the manifest description. The description is otherwise reasonable for the stated purpose.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Remove competitor/alternative tool references from the skill manifest description. Capability descriptions should focus solely on what the skill does, not redirect to other tools.

- **🔵 LOW** `LLM_SUPPLY_CHAIN_ATTACK` — Unpinned Package Installation
  > The skill instructs installation of pyopenms without a pinned version number ('uv pip install pyopenms'). This creates a supply chain risk where a future compromised or malicious version of the package could be installed. The pyopenms package is a legitimate scientific library, but unpinned installs are a security best practice concern.
  > File: `SKILL.md`
  > **Remediation:** Pin the package to a specific known-good version, e.g., 'uv pip install pyopenms==3.1.0', and consider verifying the package hash after installation.
